The Three Types of Indication: Mutabaqa, Tadammun, and Iltizam
Author defines the three types of indication of words onto meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of word-to-meaning indication (mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam) and identify each in examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Words indicate meanings in three ways: the whole, a part, or a necessary concomitant.

Understanding how words convey meaning is foundational to every Islamic science. Before a faqih can derive a ruling, he must know what a word indicates. The Islamic logicians classified indication into three precise types. This passage from the commentators explains each type with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines the types of dalala (indication) of words onto meanings. Start by noting that the word 'dalala' itself is the subject being analyzed into three parts.
2
Extract the definition of mutabaqa
Mutabaqa is the indication of the word onto the entire meaning for which it was coined. Example: 'human' indicates rational animal (the full essence).
Think of it as the whole – the complete meaning.
3
Extract the definition of tadammun
Tadammun is indication onto a part of that meaning. Example: 'human' indicates only animal or only rational, not both together.
The part is included within the whole meaning.
4
Extract the definition of iltizam
Iltizam is indication onto something outside the essence but necessarily concomitant. Example: 'roof' indicates wall (roof cannot exist without wall).
The concomitant is not part of the original meaning, but necessarily follows it.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the word 'salat' (prayer). By mutabaqa it indicates the entire act of prayer. By tadammun it indicates components like ruku' and sujud. By iltizam it indicates the obligation to perform it correctly.
Practice distinguishing these using other Islamic terms like 'zakat', 'sawm'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أولاً ـ دلالة الألفاظ على المعاني : هو أيضاً ما يبدو لنا في كتب الشراح الإسلاميين من الأبحاث اللفظية ، ويضع الشراح الإسلاميون ـ رواقيين كانوا أو مشائين ـ متقدمين كانوا أو متأخرين ـ هذا التقسيم في مقدمة أبحاثهم ـ ما عدا بعض المناطقة القلائل أمثال أبي الصلت الداني في كتابه تقويم الذهن(١) فإنه لم يبحث في دلالة الألفاظ على المعاني ، ويتكون هذا البحث في إيجاز مما يأتي : دلالة اللفظ على المعنى ينظر إليها من ثلاثة أوجه . أولاً ـ دلالة المطابقة ـ وهي دلالة اللفظ على المعنى الذي وضع له كدلالة الإنسان على الحيوان الناطق ودلالة البيت على مجموع الجدار والسقف . ثانياً ـ دلالة التضمن وهي دلالة على جزء من أجزاء المعنى المتضمن له كدلالة الإنسان على الحيوان أو على الناطق وحده وكدلالة البيت على الجدار أو السقف . ثالثاً ـ دلالة الالتزام ـ وهي استتباع أمر يكون ملازماً له ولكن خارجاً في الوقت عينه عن ذاته ـ كدلالة السقف على الجدار والمخلوق على الخالق . ويسمي السهروردي دلالة المطابقة بدلالة القصد والتضمن بالحيطة والالتزام بالتطفل . توسع الشراح الإسلاميون وخاصة المتأخرون منهم في هذه الأبحاث وبنوا عليها تفريعات لغوية كثيرة ـ ولكن ما هو المصدر ـ الذي استمد منه الإسلاميون هذا التقسيم ؟ إن من الثابت أن المنطق الأرسططاليسي لم يعرفه ، كما لم يعرفه منطق الشراح اليونانيين . أمامنا إذن إحتمالات ثلاثة : أولها ، أن الإسلاميين ابتدعوا هم تحت تأثيرات لغوية هذا التقسيم . ثانيها ، أنهم استمدوا هذا البحث من (١) أبو الصلت الداني . تقويم الذهن ( طبعة مدريد سنة ١٩١٥ ) لم يذكر الداني في كتابه مباحث الألفاظ ومباحث الجزئي والكلي والذاتي والعرضي ـ ولم يتكلم في قوانين الحد . ٤٦
First: The indication of words onto meanings: This is also what appears to us in the books of Islamic commentators from the verbal studies. And the Islamic commentators — Stoics or Peripatetics, early or late — place this division at the beginning of their studies — except for a few logicians like Abu al-Salt al-Dani in his book Taqwim al-Dhihn (1), for he did not discuss the indication of words onto meanings. And this study consists briefly of the following: The indication of the word onto the meaning is viewed from three aspects. First: indication of conformity (al-mutabaqa) — it is the indication of the word onto the meaning for which it was coined, like the indication of 'human' onto the rational animal, and the indication of 'house' onto the aggregate of wall and roof. Second: indication of inclusion (al-tadammun) — it is the indication onto a part of the parts of the meaning that includes it, like the indication of 'human' onto animal or onto the rational alone, and the indication of 'house' onto the wall or the roof. Third: indication of commitment (al-iltizam) — it is the entailment of a matter that is concomitant to it but at the same time outside its essence — like the indication of roof onto wall, and the created onto the Creator. And al-Suhrawardi calls the indication of conformity the indication of intention (al-qasd), inclusion as encompassing (al-ihata), and commitment as parasitical (al-tatfuf). The Islamic commentators — especially the later ones — expanded these studies and built upon them many linguistic branches. But what is the source from which the Muslims derived this division? It is established that Aristotelian logic did not know it, nor did the logic of the Greek commentators. So we have three possibilities: First, that the Muslims themselves innovated this division under linguistic influences. Second, that they derived this study from (1) Abu al-Salt al-Dani. Taqwim al-Dhihn (Madrid edition, 1915) — al-Dani did not mention in his book the studies of words, nor the studies of the particular and universal, the essential and accidental, and he did not speak about the rules of definition. 46
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the first aspect of studying words: the indication (dalala) of words onto meanings. The author notes that nearly all Islamic commentators, whether Stoic or Peripatetic, early or late, include this division at the start of their works, with the rare exception of Abu al-Salt al-Dani. The core of the discussion is the three types of indication. First, indication of conformity (dalala al-mutabaqa) occurs when a word signifies the entire meaning for which it was coined; for example, 'human' indicates the complete essence of rational animal, and 'house' indicates the entire structure of wall and roof. Second, indication of inclusion (dalala al-tadammun) occurs when a word signifies a part of that meaning; for instance, 'human' indicates only 'animal' or only 'rational', and 'house' indicates only 'wall' or only 'roof'. Third, indication of commitment (dalala al-iltizam) occurs when a word signifies something necessarily concomitant to the meaning but external to its essence; for example, 'roof' indicates 'wall' (since a roof requires a wall), and 'created' indicates 'Creator' (since creation implies a creator). The author then mentions that al-Suhrawardi used alternative terms: al-qasd (intention) for mutabaqa, al-ihata (encompassing) for tadammun, and al-tatfuf (parasitical) for iltizam. Finally, the passage explores the origin of this classification, noting that it is not found in Aristotle or the Greek commentators, leaving three possibilities: Islamic innovation, derivation from some unknown source, or adaptation from linguistic studies.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Checkpoint

B) Tadammun
C) Iltizam
D) None

Key Takeaway
The three types of indication—conformity, inclusion, and commitment—form the basic framework for understanding how words convey meanings in Islamic logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Noun (Ism) According to Logicians
Author provides the logical definition of noun as a word indicating meaning by convention without time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three characteristics of the logical definition of a noun and distinguish it from the grammatical definition.

Core Idea
The logical noun is timeless, conventional, and its parts signify nothing alone.

Logicians define the noun (ism) in a precise way that differs from the grammarian's definition. This distinction is crucial for understanding how terms function in arguments. In this lesson, we will break down the logical definition and apply it to examples from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three defining characteristics
Extract from the passage: (a) signifies by convention, (b) stripped of time, (c) no part indicates anything in isolation.
These three together distinguish the noun from verbs and particles.
2
Understand 'by convention'
Contrast with natural signification. The connection between word and meaning is arbitrary and agreed upon by language speakers.
For example, 'kataba' (he wrote) naturally relates to writing? Actually no, it's also conventional. But logicians emphasize this against onomatopoeia.
3
Understand 'stripped of time'
Nouns do not indicate past, present, or future; verbs do. For example, 'al-insan' does not tell you when.
Some words like 'today' refer to time but are grammatically nouns; logically they are still timeless in the sense of not having tense.
4
Understand 'no part indicates anything in isolation'
The parts of a noun (letters or syllables) do not individually convey part of the meaning. Example: 'al-insan' – the 'i' does not mean anything.
This excludes compound expressions where parts have meaning, like 'Abdullah' (servant of Allah) – but that's considered a proper noun, still a noun.
5
Apply to a new Islamic term
Test the word 'al-'ilm' (knowledge). Does it signify by convention? Yes. Is it stripped of time? Yes. Do its parts 'a', 'l', 'm' indicate anything? No.
This confirms 'al-'ilm' fits the logical definition of a noun.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما موجز تقسيم اللفظ في نفسه فهو أن « الإسم لفظة دالة بتواطىء مجردة عن الزمان وليس واحد من أجزائها دالاً على الإنفراد »(٤) أي أن الإسم لا يدل أية دلالة على زمان من الأزمنة الثلاثة كما أن أي جزء من أجزائه لا يدل على شيء على الإطلاق ، ففي قولنا « الإنسان » لا يوجد جزء من أجزائه يراد به جزء من المعنى إذا كان المقصود بهذا اللفظ تحديدنا لجملة لفظ إنسان .
As for the summary of the division of the word in itself: it is that 'The noun is a word indicating by convention, stripped of time, and none of its parts indicates in isolation.' That is, the noun does not indicate any time among the three times, and no part of it indicates anything at all. For example, in our saying 'al-insan' (the human), there is no part of its parts intended to be part of the meaning if the intended meaning of this word is our specification of the entire word 'insan'.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage defines the noun (ism) as used by logicians. It is a word that signifies by convention (bi-tawatu', i.e., by mutual agreement), is abstracted from time (meaning it does not indicate past, present, or future), and no part of it indicates any part of the meaning when considered separately. The author illustrates with the word 'al-insan' (the human): none of its letters or syllables individually convey any part of the concept of 'human'. This distinguishes the noun from the verb (which indicates time) and from compound expressions.

Key Terms

بتواطىء
مجردة عن الزمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A noun, for logicians, is a conventional word that indicates a meaning without reference to time and without its parts signifying independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of Kalima and Its Distinction from Grammatical Verb
Author defines verb in logic and compares it to the grammatical verb, noting exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which Arabic verbs qualify as logical kalima according to Ibn Sina and explain why first/second person present verbs are excluded.

Core Idea
In logic, a verb must be simple; first/second person present tense verbs are compound and thus disqualified.

Ibn Sina defines the logical term 'kalima' as a simple word indicating both a meaning and its time by convention. This definition differs from the grammatical verb (fi'l). In this lesson, you will learn the precise logical criteria and apply them to Arabic verb forms, understanding why some grammatical verbs are not logical verbs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of kalima
Find the two key components in the Arabic: 'lafz mufrad' (simple word), and that it indicates both the meaning and its time by convention (bi al-wad'). Notice the condition of simplicity is explicit.
The definition is: 'kalima is a simple word that indicates by convention the meaning and the time of that meaning.'
2
Compare kalima with grammatical fi'l
Ibn Sina asserts that not every grammatical verb (fi'l) is a logical kalima. The grammatical verb includes all tenses and persons, but logic imposes additional constraints.
This highlights the precision of logical terminology over grammatical categories.
3
Examine the exception: present tense non-third person
The present tense verbs for first and second person (e.g., 'I do', 'you do') are considered compound because they include an implicit pronoun. Therefore they are not simple, and thus not kalima.
Only past tense and third person present verbs are simple in logic.
4
Apply the rule to Arabic verb forms
Given an Arabic verb, check if it is mufrad (simple) and indicates a time. Past tense (e.g., ذهب) is simple; third person present (يذهب) is simple; first person present (أذهب) is compound due to the implied pronoun 'أنا'. Decide accordingly.
This classification affects how verbs are used in logical propositions and syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلمة فهي لفظ مفرد يدل بالوضع على المعنى وعلى زمان ذلك المعنى . ثم يقارن ابن سينا بين الكلمة عند المناطقة والفعل عند النحاة ويقرر أنه ليس من المحتم أن يكون كل فعل عند النحاة كلمة عند المناطقة لأن المضارع غير الغائب أي المتكلم والمخاطب فعل النحاة ولا يعتبر كلمة عند المناطقة ، لأن المضارع غير الغائب مركب والمركب غير كلمة(١) .
As for the word (kalima), it is a simple word that indicates by convention the meaning and the time of that meaning. Then Ibn Sina compares the word (kalima) among logicians and the verb (fi'l) among grammarians and decides that it is not necessary that every verb among grammarians be a word (kalima) among logicians, because the present tense other than the third person, i.e., the first and second person, is a verb among grammarians and is not considered a word among logicians, because the present tense other than the third person is compound, and the compound is not a word (kalima).
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage defines the logical term 'kalima' (often translated as 'verb' or 'word'). In logic, a kalima is a simple word that signifies by convention both a meaning and a time associated with that meaning. Ibn Sina then compares this to the grammatical verb (fi'l) in Arabic. He notes that not every grammatical verb qualifies as a logical kalima. Specifically, the present tense verbs in the first and second person (e.g., 'I do', 'you do') are considered verbs by grammarians but are not kalima in logic because they are compound (they include a pronoun, e.g., 'ana' or 'anta', making them composite). Only the third person present (e.g., 'he does') and past tense verbs are simple and thus qualify. This highlights the precision of logical terminology compared to grammar.

Key Terms

الفعل
المضارع غير الغائب

Key Takeaway

The Fourfold Classification of Words in Relation to Meanings
Author presents the fivefold division of words relative to meanings and its connection to Greek and Arabic linguistic studies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish between mutawati', mushtarak, mutaradif, and mutazayil using Islamic examples and explain their relevance to usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
Words relate to meanings in four essential patterns: univocal, homonymous, synonymous, heteronymous.

This lesson introduces the fifth division of logic: the classification of words based on their relationship to meanings. This classification bridges Aristotelian logic with Arabic linguistics and is foundational for usul al-fiqh, where precision in word-meaning relations determines legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
From the passage, list the four categories of words: mutawati' (univocal), mushtarak (homonymous), mutaradif (synonymous), mutazayil (heteronymous).
2
Define each category operationally
For each category, state how the word relates to its meaning(s): mutawati' shares one meaning across instances; mushtarak has multiple unrelated meanings; mutaradif has multiple words for one meaning; mutazayil has distinct words for distinct meanings.
Use the literal translation to help.
3
Provide Islamic examples
Give a concrete example from Islamic sciences for each category. For example: mutawati' = 'insan' (human) applies to all humans equally; mushtarak = 'ayn (eye, spring, essence); mutaradif = 'asaf and 'shafaq' (twilight); mutazayil = 'kurasiy' (chair) and 'maktab' (desk).
Ensure examples are from Arabic or Islamic disciplines.
4
Connect to usul al-fiqh
Explain why this classification matters in usul al-fiqh: jurists must know whether a word is univocal or homonymous to interpret legal texts correctly. For instance, if a word is mushtarak, they must seek context or preference (tarjih).
Recall the author's remark about the great connection with usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نرى من هذا أن الشراح الإسلاميين يحاولون أن يدخلوا بتلك التقاسيم الأرسططاليسية في صميم المسائل اللغوية - ودعا استناد هذه الأبحاث إلى اللغة اليونانية أن قام من علماء أهل السنة من نادى بأن المنطق اليوناني يستند إلى خصائص اللغة اليونانية فهو منطقها - ومن ثمة فلا حاجة للمسلمين به - لأن تراكيب العربية تخالف تراكيب اليونانية وهذا الاعتراض وجيه إلى حد كبير يدل على عمق فهم المسلمين لما بين اللغة اليونانية والمنطق اليوناني من صلة . التقسيم الخامس ، في نسبة الألفاظ إلى المعاني - وهذا التقسيم أثر وتأثر بالدراسات اللغوية العربية مع أنه في جوهره أرسططاليسي . فالألفاظ تنقسم من حيث نسبتها إلى المعاني إلى المتواطىء والمشترك والمترادف والمتزايل وقد توسع الإسلاميون في هذا البحث ، لكن لا في جوهره بل في ابتداع أقسام جديدة يمكن ردها ببساطة إلى الأقسام الرئيسية التي وضعها أرسطو . هذا من ناحية - ومن ناحية أخرى إن لهذا البحث صلة كبيرة بالأبحاث الأصولية .
We see from this that the Islamic commentators attempt to insert these Aristotelian divisions into the core of linguistic matters. And the reliance of these investigations on the Greek language caused some Sunni scholars to proclaim that Greek logic is based on the characteristics of the Greek language, so it is its logic; and therefore Muslims have no need for it, because the structures of Arabic differ from the structures of Greek. This objection is largely valid, indicating the depth of Muslim understanding of the connection between the Greek language and Greek logic. The fifth division: concerning the relationship of words to meanings. This division was influenced by and influenced Arabic linguistic studies, although in its essence it is Aristotelian. Words are divided according to their relationship to meanings into: univocal (mutawati'), homonymous (mushtarak), synonymous (mutaradif), and heteronymous (mutazayil). The Islamic scholars expanded this study, but not in its essence; rather, in creating new divisions that can be simply reduced to the main divisions established by Aristotle. On the one hand, this. On the other hand, this investigation has a great connection with the investigations of usul al-fiqh.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage opens by noting that Islamic commentators attempted to integrate Aristotelian categories into linguistic discussions. Some Sunni scholars objected that Greek logic is inherently tied to the Greek language and therefore unsuitable for Arabic. The author considers this a valid objection. Then the fifth division is introduced: the classification of words in relation to their meanings, a topic influenced by both Greek logic and Arabic linguistics. The four categories are: univocal (a single meaning), homonymous (multiple unrelated meanings), synonymous (multiple words for one meaning), and heteronymous (distinct words for distinct meanings). Islamic scholars expanded this classification but did not alter its Aristotelian core; any new subdivisions can be reduced to Aristotle's original divisions. Finally, the author points out the strong relevance of this topic to the science of usul al-fiqh, signaling the practical importance of these logical distinctions in Islamic legal theory.

Key Terms
المتواطىء
المشترك
المترادف

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of words in relation to meanings is an essential logical tool for precise interpretation in usul al-fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Synonymy as a Definitional Tool in Islamic Logic
Synonymy as a type of definition: middle position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when synonymy is used as a type of definition (hadd) in Islamic logic and explain the middle position of some theologians.

Core Idea
Synonymy is not redundancy; it is a legitimate logical tool for definition.

The passage presents a middle ground between extreme denial of synonymy (al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi) and uncritical acceptance. Some theologians considered synonymic terms as a type of definition (hadd), where the clear term explains the obscure one. This lesson unpacks that definitional use of synonymy and its place in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the extreme positions on synonymy
Find al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi's denial of synonymy and the opposing view. Why would someone deny synonymy entirely?
2
Understand the middle position
Some theologians considered synonyms as a type of definition: the clear term explains the obscure. This is a compromise.
3
Connect synonymy to the concept of hadd
In logic, hadd normally means definition by genus and differentia. Here it is extended to include explanation via synonym. Why is this allowed?
4
Apply this to a fiqh example
Suppose you need to define 'riba' for a student. Could you use a synonymous term like 'ziyada' (increase) to clarify? What are the limits of this method?

يتضمن تعريف المعرف وهو خلاف الأصل . وأنكر الحكيم الترمذي - وهو صوفي ممتاز - الترادف في كتاب له على جانب من الأهمية هو « الفروق ومنع الترادف »(۱) . حل بعض المتكلمين المسألة حلاً وسطاً . فالألفاظ المترادفة هي ألفاظ يشرح بعضها بعضاً ، الجلي منها يشرح الخفي - فهي ليست إلا نوعاً من الحد - لأن الحد هو تبديل لفظ خفي بلفظ أوضح منه تنبيهاً للسائل (۲) . وتتعرض أيضاً اللفظ المشترك لما تعرض له اللفظ المترادف - هل نجيزه أم لا نجيزه ، هل نتوصل به إلى المقصود أم لا نتوصل ، هل كلمة قرؤ مشتركة بين الحيض والطهر ، أم إنها لواحد منهما فقط ؟ اختلف الأصوليون في هذا اختلافاً شديداً (۳) . يمكننا أن نقرر إذن : أن مسألة المترادف والمشترك كانت على جانب كبير من الأهمية لدى علماء الأصول . ومما لا شك فيه أن الأصوليين الأرسططاليسيين . أي الذين كتبوا مقدمات كلامية ، في أول أبحاثهم تأثروا بالمنطق الارسططاليسي . ولكن ليس معنى هذا أن هذا التقسيم كان غريباً عن المسلمين . إننا لا نستطيع أن ننكر وجود أسماء مترادفة ومشتركة ومتزايلة في لغة من اللغات ، ولكن الأصوليين الارسططاليسيين اكتسبوا من أرسطو إستقرار التقسيم واتجاههم به إتجاهاً منطقياً . إلى هنا تنتهي مباحث الألفاظ من منطق الشراح الإسلاميين . وقد تبين ما قلناه من أن الشراح الإسلاميين لم يقفوا عند حد العناصر الارسططاليسية ، بل أضافوا أبحاثاً خاصة بهم وأبحاثاً أخرى رواقية ، ومزجوا هذين العنصرين بالعنصر الارسططاليسي ، ويكون هذا المزيج دائماً منطق الشراح الإسلاميين .
It includes the definition of the defined [term] which is contrary to the original. Al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi—an eminent Sufi—denied synonymy in a book of his of some importance, namely 'Al-Furuq wa Man' al-Taraduf' (1). Some theologians solved the problem in a middle way. Synonymic terms are terms that explain each other; the clear among them explains the obscure—they are but a type of definition, because definition is the replacement of an obscure term with a clearer one to alert the questioner (2). The homonymic term also encountered what the synonymic term encountered: do we allow it or not? Do we reach the intended [meaning] through it or not? Is the word 'quru' homonymous between menstruation and purity, or is it for only one of them? The usul scholars differed greatly on this (3). We can then state: the issue of synonymy and homonymy was of great importance to the scholars of usul. There is no doubt that the Aristotelian usul scholars—i.e., those who wrote theological introductions at the beginning of their researches—were influenced by Aristotelian logic. But this does not mean that this division was foreign to Muslims. We cannot deny the existence of synonymous, homonymous, and heteronymous names in any language, but the Aristotelian usul scholars acquired from Aristotle the stabilization of the division and their direction of it in a logical direction. Here end the topics of words from the logic of the Islamic commentators. And what we have said has become clear: the Islamic commentators did not stop at the limit of Aristotelian elements, but added their own researches and other Stoic researches, and mixed these two elements with the Aristotelian element, and this mixture is always the logic of the Islamic commentators.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by the author of how synonymy (taraduf) and homonymy (ishtirak) were treated by Islamic scholars, particularly usul scholars, and the extent to which this treatment was indebted to Aristotelian logic. The author begins by mentioning al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi, an eminent Sufi who denied synonymy altogether in his book 'Al-Furuq wa Man' al-Taraduf'. This represents an extreme position. In contrast, some theologians adopted a middle position: they considered synonymous terms as a type of definition (hadd), where the clearer term explains the obscure one. This shows that synonymy was not seen as a defect but as a tool for clarification. The passage then turns to homonymy, raising the same questions of permissibility and effectiveness. The author gives the example of the word 'quru', which in Arabic can mean both menstruation and the period of purity (tuhr), and notes that usul scholars differed intensely on whether it is truly homonymous or specific to one meaning. The author then concludes that the issue was very important in usul, and while Aristotelian usul scholars were influenced by Aristotelian logic, the division into synonym/homonym was not foreign to Muslims—languages naturally have such categories. However, Aristotle provided a stabilization and logical direction. Finally, the author summarizes that Islamic commentators did not limit themselves to Aristotelian elements; they added their own and Stoic elements, creating a unique synthesis. This passage thus defends the originality and eclecticism of Islamic logic against claims of pure Aristotelian borrowing.

مترادف
حد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the middle solution proposed by some theologians regarding synonymous terms?
A) They denied synonymy entirely, following al-Hakim al-Tirmidhi.
B) They considered synonyms as a type of definition, where the clear explains the obscure.
C) They argued that all synonyms are homonyms.
D) They avoided the issue entirely.

Key Takeaway
In Islamic logic, synonymy can serve as a legitimate definitional tool when the clear term explains the obscure, bridging language and logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a term in usul al-fiqh that is often defined using synonymy (e.g., 'qiyas' defined as 'al-istidlal'). Explain why this is considered a type of hadd in the middle position discussed in the passage.

Homonymy (Ishtirak) and Its Role in Usul al-Fiqh
Homonymy in usul: permissibility and resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish homonymy from synonymy and explain the debate about homonymous terms in usul al-fiqh, using the example of 'quru'.

Core Idea
Homonymy forces usul scholars to decide which meaning is intended—a critical step in deriving rulings.

The passage raises key questions about homonymy: is it allowed? Can we reach the intended meaning? The example of 'quru' (menstruation or purity) shows how homonymy directly impacts legal interpretation. This lesson explores the debate and its logical underpinnings.

Guided Path
1
Define homonymy (mushtarak) vs synonymy
Homonymy: one term with multiple meanings. Synonymy: multiple terms for one meaning. How does each affect communication?
2
Identify the questions about homonymy
The passage asks: do we allow it? Can we reach the intended? These are practical questions for usul scholars when interpreting texts.
3
Analyze the example of 'quru'
The word 'quru' in Arabic can mean menstruation or the period between menses (tuhr). Usul scholars differed: is it truly homonymous or does it have one primary meaning?
4
Connect to usul methodology
How does a usul scholar resolve homonymy? By context (qarina), frequency, or other indicators. This is part of the science of al-mushtarak.

Islamic Logic's Originality: Beyond Aristotelian Influence
Critical argument for Islamic logic's originality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that Islamic logic is not merely Aristotelian but a synthesis with Stoic and original elements.

Core Idea
Islamic logic is a unique synthesis—not a mere copy of Aristotle.

The passage defends the originality of Islamic logic. While Aristotelian usul scholars were influenced by Greek logic, the division of words into synonym and homonym was natural to Arabic. Moreover, Islamic commentators added their own and Stoic elements, creating a distinct tradition. This lesson examines the critical argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being criticized
The implicit claim is that Islamic logic is purely Aristotelian. The author argues against this by showing natural presence of synonymy/homonymy in all languages.
2
Find the author's evidence
The author points out that any language has synonyms and homonyms. The Islamic scholars did not need Aristotle for this division; they stabilized it logically.
3
Understand the 'expansion probe'
The passage states that Islamic commentators added their own researches and Stoic elements, mixing them with Aristotelian ones. This is an expansion beyond Greek sources.
4
Evaluate the argument's strength
Is the author convincing? The evidence of natural language is strong, but the extent of Aristotelian influence on the logical framework remains debated.

Nominalist Critique of Aristotelian Definition in Islam
Author presents the Stoic nominalist view, its adoption by Islamic theologians, and the consequence for Aristotelian definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the nominalist critique of Aristotelian definition (hadd) and identify its proponents among Islamic theologians.

Core Idea
If universals are mere names, definitions based on genus and species collapse.

The Stoics denied that universals exist outside the mind—only individuals are real. This nominalist position was adopted by many Islamic theologians, including Fakhr al-Din al-Razi and al-Suhrawardi, who concluded that the Aristotelian definition (hadd), which rests on the universals of genus and species, is invalid. In this lesson, we trace this critical line and its implications for Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the Stoic position on universals
From the Brochard quotations, note that the Stoics considered general ideas as mere names, denied the logical value of genus/species divisions, and held that only individuals exist.
The Stoics are early nominalists.
2
Recognize the Islamic adoption
The author claims the majority of mutakallimun are Stoic on this point, with al-Razi denying the universal absolutely.
Al-Razi's denial is explicit.
3
Understand the consequence for hadd
Al-Razi declared the definition composed of genus and species invalid. Al-Suhrawardi also denied it, and Ibn Taymiyya used this nominalism to critique Aristotelian logic.
Hadd is the Aristotelian definition.
4
Contrast with the usuliyyin response
Some Aristotelian legal theorists (usuliyyun) tried to redefine genus and species rather than reject definitions entirely.
This shows a spectrum of reactions.

يقول الأستاذ Brochard « إن من المسلم به أن الرواقيين تابعوا أنتستانس في أن الأفكار العامة - أي التصورات - ليست إلا أسماء، فلا يوجد في الواقع إلا الأفراد، أما الكلي فلا يوجد على الإطلاق » وفي فقرة أخرى « لم يوافق الرواقيون على تقسيم الموجودات إلى أجناس وأنواع. ولو أنهم لم يجهلوا هذا التقسيم لأنهم في تقسيمهم للموجودات ذكروا الجنس الأعلى - ولكن ليست لهذه التقاسيم أية قيمة في المنطق. وما يميز الطبيعة الخاصة لكل موجود ليس هو عنصراً مشتركاً في كل الموجودات بل صفة فردية ومادية. وعلى العموم لا يوجد تشابه بين الموجودات - ولكن يوجد أفراد فقط» وفي فقرة ثالثة يقول « إن المشكلة التي تقابل الباحثين في فلسفة أرسطو - وهي: إنه لا يوجد في الواقع إلا العلم الكلي، الشخص أو المعين هو الذي يوجد فقط ـ عالجها الرواقيون: إنهم لم يقبلوا الشرط الأول، واستبقوا الشطر الأخير». هل أخذ الإسلاميون بالمذهب الرواقي؟ إن غالبية المتكلمين رواقيون في هذه النقطة. يذهبون إلى أن الماهية ليست بشيء على الإطلاق - وأن الكلي لا وجود له في الخارج - إنما الكليات أسماء تستخدم في التخاطب فحسب. وإن البحث في كتب فخر الدين الرازي ليثبت هذا تمام الإثبات. قد أنكر فخر الدين الرازي وجود الكلي إطلاقاً. ولكن أي النتائج ستنتج عن إنكار الكليات الخارجية. سينتج عن هذا إنكار الحد الأرسططاليسي القائم على الكليين الجنس والفصل. وصل الإسلاميون الرواقيون إلى هذه النتيجة أيضاً. فالرازي يقول: الحد مركب من الجنس والفصل عرف بطلانه في المنطق. وأنكر السهروردي الحد الأرسططاليسي القائم على فكرة الماهية أي الجنس والفصل. وسنرى نقد السهروردي هذا بعد. وسنرى أيضاً ابن تيمية يستخدم هذا المذهب الإسمي الذي ينكر وجود الكليات في الخارج ويطبقه في كثير من نقده الهدمي لعناصر المنطق الأرسططاليسي. يمكننا الآن أن نجزم لا بمعرفة المسلمين للمنطق الرواقي فحسب، بل وبأخذ كثيرين منهم بهذا المنطق. النوع والجنس في مدرسة الأصوليين الأرسططاليسيين: لم يقبل بعض الأصوليين الأرسططاليسيين فكرة النوع والجنس كما تركها أرسطو وفورفوريوس، بل حاولوا وضع تعاريف جديدة لهذين الكليين.
Professor Brochard says: 'It is agreed that the Stoics followed Antisthenes in that general ideas—that is, concepts—are nothing but names; so in reality only individuals exist, while the universal does not exist at all.' And in another paragraph: 'The Stoics did not agree to the division of existents into genera and species. Even if they were not ignorant of this division, because in their division of existents they mentioned the highest genus, but these divisions have no value in logic. What distinguishes the particular nature of each existent is not a common element in all existents, but an individual and material quality. In general, there is no similarity between existents—only individuals exist.' And in a third paragraph he says: 'The problem that faces researchers in Aristotle's philosophy—namely, that only universal knowledge exists in reality, while the individual or particular is what exists only—the Stoics dealt with it: they did not accept the first condition, and retained the last part.' Did the Muslims adopt the Stoic doctrine? The majority of theologians are Stoics on this point. They hold that quiddity (mahiyya) is nothing at all—and that the universal has no existence in the external world—rather, universals are names used only in communication. Research in the books of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi confirms this fully. Al-Razi denied the existence of the universal absolutely. But what results will result from the denial of external universals? This will result in the denial of the Aristotelian definition (hadd) based on the two universals: genus and species. The Stoic Muslims also reached this conclusion. Al-Razi says: 'The definition composed of genus and species—its invalidity has been known in logic.' And al-Suhrawardi denied the Aristotelian definition based on the idea of quiddity, i.e., genus and species. We will see this critique of al-Suhrawardi later. And we will also see Ibn Taymiyya using this nominalist doctrine that denies the existence of universals in the external world and applying it in much of his destructive critique of the elements of Aristotelian logic. We can now assert not only the Muslims' knowledge of Stoic logic, but also the adoption of this logic by many of them. Species and genus in the school of the Aristotelian legal theorists (usuliyyin): Some Aristotelian legal theorists did not accept the concept of species and genus as left by Aristotle and Porphyry; rather, they attempted to put forward new definitions for these two universals.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage presents the Stoic rejection of external universals and its reception in Islamic thought. It opens with three quotations from the French scholar Brochard summarizing the Stoic position: that general ideas are mere names, only individuals exist; the Stoics rejected the logical value of divisions into genera and species, emphasizing individual qualities; and they solved the Aristotelian problem of universal knowledge versus individual existence by denying universal reality and retaining particulars. The text then asks whether Muslims adopted this view, answering that the majority of Islamic theologians (mutakallimun) are Stoic on this point: they consider quiddity (mahiyya) to be nothing and universals as mere names. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi is cited as a key figure who denied the universal absolutely. The consequence is the denial of the Aristotelian definition (hadd) which is composed of genus and species, both universals. Al-Razi explicitly declared such definitions invalid. Al-Suhrawardi also denied the Aristotelian definition, and Ibn Taymiyya applied this nominalist doctrine in his critique of Aristotelian logic. The passage concludes that Muslims not only knew Stoic logic but many adopted it. Finally, it introduces a new section on the legal theorists (usuliyyin) who, while Aristotelian, did not accept the traditional Aristotelian-Porphyrian concepts of genus and species and sought to redefine them.

Introduction to the Study of Definition (Ta'rif)
Author outlines the importance of definition and previews three key topics to be covered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the purpose of real definition (hadd haqiqi) and identify its key components (genus and differentia) as outlined in the Islamic logical tradition.

Core Idea
Definition is the heart of concept formation and bridges logic and metaphysics.

This lesson places definition at the centre of the study of concepts (tasawwurat). The author explains that earlier discussions on expression and meaning were merely preparatory. Here we learn the three key topics: definition's link to Aristotle's logic and metaphysics, its divisions, and the methods to obtain definitions. Understanding definition is essential for precise reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the place of definition
Note that definition is the core of tasawwurat, and the previous topics were preliminaries.
2
Grasp the purpose of real definition
Understand that a real definition (hadd haqiqi) aims to indicate the essence (mahiyya) of a thing through its essential attributes: genus (jins) and differentia (fasl).
3
Connect definition to logic and metaphysics
See how definition straddles both logic (as a tool for concept formation) and metaphysics (as a way to grasp essences).
4
Preview the three topics
The author will cover: (1) definition's link to Aristotle's logic and metaphysics, (2) divisions of definition, (3) methods leading to definition.

Definition's Dual Nature: Logic and Metaphysics in Aristotle and Islam
Author explains how definition straddles logic and metaphysics in Aristotle's framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why definition by real essence (al-had al-haqiqi) ties logic to metaphysics in Aristotle's framework and how this affected Islamic scholars.

Core Idea
Definition is not purely logical; it reveals quiddity, making it metaphysical.

This passage from the Manahij al-Bahth explains a foundational tension in Aristotelian logic: definition straddles logic and metaphysics. You will see how real definition relies on quiddity and causes, and how this connection led Islamic thinkers like Avicenna to reassess the boundaries of logic. Understanding this prepares you for why later Islamic logicians sometimes rejected Aristotelian definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects of definition's oscillation
The author says definition oscillates between logic and metaphysics from two aspects. Find these two aspects in the passage.
One relates to quiddity, the other to causes.
2
Understand the first aspect: definition aims at quiddity via essential attributes
The purpose of real definition is to arrive at the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing by obtaining its essential attributes (genus and differentia). This makes definition inherently metaphysical, because quiddity is a metaphysical concept.
3
Understand the second aspect: definition depends on the theory of causes
Real definition relies on the study of causes (al-'ilal), which is a metaphysical and physical investigation. Aristotle emphasized this connection, and Muslim Peripatetic commentators followed him.
The four causes are material, formal, efficient, and final.
4
See the Islamic response
Islamic scholars reacted to this connection: Avicenna removed the categories from logic to metaphysics, and theologians and legal theorists rejected the Aristotelian definition precisely because of its metaphysical grounding. This shows how the tension shaped Islamic logical thought.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقطة الأولى : صلة التعريف بالمنطق وما بعد الطبيعة عند أرسطو . تردد مبحث التعريف بين المنطق والميتافيزيقا الأرسططاليسيين واضح تماماً من ناحيتين . الناحية الأولى إذا ما بحثنا في الغاية من التعريف بالحد الحقيقي عند أرسطو ، فان ما يقصد من تعريف الشيء ، هو التوصل إلى الماهية أو الكنه بواسطة الحصول على الصفات الذاتية له - أي الجنس والفصل ، لأن التعريف بالحد الحقيقي هو الدال على ماهية الشيء . ومجرد استناد التعريف بالحد الحقيقي إلى الماهية يثبت صلة التعريف الوثيقة بالميتافيزيقا الأرسططاليسية وترددها كتبهم . هذا من ناحية ، ومن ناحية أخرى يثبت اتصال مبحث الحد بالمباحث الميتافيزيقية والفيزيقية عند أرسطو ، ثم عند الشراح المشائين من بعده ، استناد الحد الحقيقي على مبحث العلل . فقد عني أرسطو بتوضيح الصلة بين الحد ومبحث العلل ، وتابعه الإسلاميون متابعة تامة . صلة التعريف أو صلة المنطق على العموم بما بعد الطبيعة مسألة كان لها دور هام في المدارس الإسلامية المختلفة . وقد رأينا ابن سينا من قبل يحاول أن يخرج من نطاق المنطق الأرسططاليسي مبحث المقولات ، لأنه من أبحاث الميتافيزيقا ، وسنرى المتكلمين والأصوليين لا يوافقون على التعريف الأرسططاليسي لاتصاله بالميتافيزيقا واستناده إليها . بل إنهم سينقضون كثيراً من مباحث المنطق الأرسططاليسي لتلك الفكرة بعينها .
The first point: The connection of definition to logic and metaphysics in Aristotle. The oscillation of the topic of definition between Aristotelian logic and metaphysics is clear from two aspects. The first aspect: if we examine the purpose of definition by real essence according to Aristotle, the aim of defining a thing is to arrive at the quiddity or essence by obtaining its essential attributes—i.e., genus and differentia—because definition by real essence is what indicates the quiddity of a thing. The mere reliance of definition by real essence on quiddity establishes the close connection of definition to Aristotelian metaphysics and its oscillation in their books. This from one aspect, and from another aspect it establishes the connection of the topic of definition to metaphysical and physical investigations in Aristotle, and after him among the Peripatetic commentators, due to the reliance of real definition on the topic of causes. Aristotle was concerned to clarify the connection between definition and the topic of causes, and the Muslims followed him fully. The connection of definition, or of logic in general, to metaphysics was an important issue in different Islamic schools. We have previously seen Avicenna trying to remove the topic of categories from the scope of Aristotelian logic because it belongs to metaphysical investigations, and we will see the theologians and legal theorists not agreeing with the Aristotelian definition because of its connection to metaphysics and reliance upon it. Rather, they will refute many of the Aristotelian logical investigations for that very idea.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains the ambiguous relationship between definition (al-ta'rif) and metaphysics in Aristotle's system, and how this impacted Islamic logical thought. The author begins by stating that definition oscillates between logic and metaphysics from two angles. First, the purpose of real definition (al-had al-haqiqi) is to arrive at quiddity (al-mahiyya) by identifying essential attributes (genus and differentia), which makes definition inherently metaphysical. Second, real definition relies on the theory of causes (al-'ilal), which is a metaphysical and physical study. The author notes that Aristotle himself clarified this connection, and Muslim commentators followed him. Then the author highlights the significant role of this connection in Islamic schools: Avicenna removed the categories from logic to metaphysics, and theologians and legal theorists rejected the Aristotelian definition precisely because of its metaphysical grounding. The passage thus prepares the reader to understand why definition was a contested topic.

Key Terms
الحد الحقيقي (al-had al-haqiqi): The definition that indicates the quiddity of a thing by its essential attributes (genus and differentia). [Note: In later Islamic logic, especially post-Avicenna, the term is standardized; here it is contrasted with verbal and descriptive definitions.]
الماهية (al-mahiyya): The quiddity or essence of a thing, what it is. [Note: Stable across the tradition, but its metaphysical implications are emphasized here.]
الجنس والفصل (al-jins wa al-fasl): Genus and differentia, the two essential components of a real definition. [Note: Standard in Aristotelian logic; no significant shift.]
العلل (al-'ilal): Causes, which real definition depends on according to Aristotle. [Note: In Peripatetic logic, the four causes are fundamental; later Islamic logicians often restrict this.]

Key Takeaway
The connection of definition to metaphysics was a key point of contention that shaped Islamic logical thought.

The Threefold Division of Definitions: Islamic Expansion on Aristotle
Author presents the threefold division of definitions and contrasts it with Aristotle's twofold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of definitions (hadd haqiqi, hadd lafzi, rasm) and trace their historical origins to Aristotle and Galen.

Core Idea
The threefold division of definitions is an Islamic innovation, not Aristotelian.

Islamic commentators expanded upon Aristotle's original twofold classification of definitions into real and verbal, adding a third type: descriptive definition (rasm). This expansion, introduced by Galen under Stoic influence, became standard in the Islamic logical tradition. Understanding these three types helps you analyze how definitions are constructed in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of definition
Locate the three types mentioned: hadd haqiqi (real essence definition), hadd lafzi (verbal definition), and rasm (descriptive definition). Note that rasm is also called al-hadd al-rasmi.
2
Compare with Aristotle's twofold division
Aristotle only recognized two: definitions that give quiddity (hadd haqiqi) and those that specify a verbal relationship (hadd lafzi). Observe that rasm is absent in his framework.
Aristotle is referred to as 'al-mu'allim al-awwal' (the First Teacher).
3
Understand the historical origin of rasm
The author states that Galen (Jalinus), influenced by Stoicism, introduced the concept of rasm. This illustrates how later scholars enriched Aristotelian logic.
Galen's influence is acknowledged but rarely mentioned in later texts.
4
Apply the division to an Islamic science example
Consider a definition from fiqh: 'Riba is an increase in a loan without a corresponding benefit.' Is this hadd haqiqi (if it captures essence) or rasm (if it describes by accident)? Analyze using the threefold division.
This step connects the lesson to real Islamic reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النقطة الثانية التي سنتكلم عنها : هي أقسام التعريف عند الشراح الإسلاميين اختلف الشراح الإسلاميون في وضع هذه الأقسام اختلافاً كبيراً . أما التقسيم الشائع بين كثيرين منهم أن التعريف ينقسم إلى التعريف بالحد الحقيقي والتعريف بالحد اللفظي والتعريف بالحد الرسمي أو الرسم وهذا التقسيم الثلاثي لم يعرفه المعلم الأول . ففكرة الرسم لم يضعها أرسطو . ولكن وضعها جالينوس متأثراً بالرواقية . أما أنواع التعريف عند أرسطو فهي : تعريف يفيد ماهية الشيء ، وتعريف لا يفيد . ولكن يعين علاقة لفظية بين المحدود والحد الذي يشير إليه . وعلى هذا الأساس أقيم التقسيم المشهور - التعريف بالحد الحقيقي والتعريف بالحد اللفظي (١) .
The second point we will discuss: the divisions of definition according to the Islamic commentators. The Islamic commentators differed greatly in establishing these divisions. As for the common division among many of them: definition is divided into definition by real essence, definition by verbal definition, and definition by descriptive definition or description. This tripartite division was not known to the First Teacher. For the idea of description was not established by Aristotle. Rather, it was established by Galen, influenced by Stoicism. As for the types of definition according to Aristotle: they are a definition that benefits the quiddity of a thing, and a definition that does not benefit, but rather specifies a verbal relationship between the defined and the definition that indicates it. On this basis, the famous division was established: definition by real essence and definition by verbal definition.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of definitions among Islamic commentators. The author notes that there is significant disagreement, but the common division is threefold: (1) definition by real essence (al-had al-haqiqi), (2) definition by verbal definition (al-had al-lafzi), and (3) definition by descriptive definition or description (al-rasm). This tripartite division, the author emphasizes, was not known to Aristotle (referred to as the First Teacher). The concept of rasm was introduced by Galen under Stoic influence. Aristotle himself only distinguished between a definition that gives the quiddity (real definition) and one that does not give the quiddity but only indicates a verbal relationship (verbal definition). The author concludes that the famous division into real and verbal definitions is based on Aristotle's original twofold classification. The lesson clarifies the historical development of definition types, showing how Islamic commentators expanded upon Aristotle's framework.

الحد الحقيقي
الحد اللفظي
الرسم
المعلم الأول
جالينوس

D) The Stoics

Key Takeaway
The threefold division of definitions (real, verbal, descriptive) is an Islamic expansion of Aristotle's twofold classification, with rasm originating from Galen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following definition from a fiqh text: 'Al-khamr (wine) is every intoxicating drink that clouds the mind.' Determine whether this is a hadd haqiqi, hadd lafzi, or rasm. Explain your reasoning using the threefold division.
?
Find a real definition from any Islamic science (fiqh, kalam, nahw) and classify it as hadd haqiqi, hadd lafzi, or rasm. Cite the text and briefly justify.

Abu al-Barakat's Threefold Classification: Hudud, Rusum, Tamthilat
Abu al-Barakat's threefold classification of defining expressions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of defining expressions according to Abu al-Barakat and evaluate their epistemological value.

Core Idea
Definitions are not all equal: only hudud yield complete essential knowledge.

Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi introduces a unique classification of definitions, adding analogies (tamthilat) as a third category alongside definitions proper (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). In this lesson, we will explore how he ranks these three types and why he considers analogies the weakest form of definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types
Find the three categories Abu al-Barakat lists: hudud (حدود), rusum (رسوم), and tamthilat (تمثيلات). Note that tamthilat is his novel addition.
2
Understand the origins
Observe that hudud are traced to Aristotle, rusum to Galen, while tamthilat is attributed to Abu al-Barakat himself.
3
Examine the hierarchy
Arrange the three types from highest to lowest based on knowledge they provide: hudud give essential (dhati) knowledge, rusum give accidental (aradi) knowledge, tamthilat give neither.
4
Analyze the function of tamthil
Read the definition of tamthil: 'defining a thing by its equivalents and similars, and the universal by its particulars.' Consider its pedagogical role: it makes meanings familiar but is not a proper definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وينزع أبو البركات البغدادي في تقسيم التعريف منزعاً آخر، فيقسم الأقاويل المعرفة إلى ثلاثة أقسام : الحدود والرسوم والتمثيلات. أما التعريف بالحد فهو عنصر أرسططاليسي، والتعريف بالرسم عنصر جاليني وقد عرضناهما من قبل. يبقى إذن التعريف بالتمثيل وهو العنصر الجديد في هذا التقسيم. ويعرفه أبو البركات : بأنه « تعريف الشيء بنظائره وأشباهه والكلي المعقول بجزئياته وأشخاصه » وينظم أبو البركات أنواع التعاريف في نظام تصاعدي ويضع في قمة هذا النظام الحد لأنه طريق المعرفة الذاتية، ثم يليه الرسم لأنه طريق المعرفة العرضية، وفي آخر هذا النظام التمثيل، لأنه لا يفيد لا معرفة ذاتية ولا عرضية. يقول أبو البركات البغدادي : « وفائدة التعريف بالتمثيل هو أنه يورد تبع الأقاويل المعرفة وهي الحدود والرسوم فيكون مفهماً لمضمونها لا متمماً لمفهومها بايناسه الذهن بما غرب عن ألفاظها وتقريبه عليه بعيد مدلولاتها وجمعاً لها متفرق معانيها وهو كثير النفع في التعاليم لتقريبه على المتعلمين، وتخفيفه عند المعلمين ولكن لا تحتاج إليه الأذهان القوية . وأفضل الأقاويل الحدود لأنها تفيد المعرفة الذاتية التامة، وأنقص منها الرسوم لأنها تفيد المعرفة العرضية أو مشوبة بالعرضية لأنها تتم الذاتية الناقصة بالعرضية المأخوذة من الأعراض واللواحق، وأقل منها كثيراً التمثيلات لأنها لا تفيد معرفة عرضية ولا ذاتية وإنما هي لتسهيل الأفادة ».
And Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi takes another approach in the division of definition, so he divides defining expressions into three sections: definitions (hudud), descriptions (rusum), and analogies (tamthilat). As for definition by hadd, it is an Aristotelian element, and definition by rasm is a Galenic element, and we have presented both previously. So there remains definition by tamthil, which is the new element in this division. And Abu al-Barakat defines it: 'It is the definition of a thing by its equivalents and similars, and the intelligible universal by its particulars and individuals.' And Abu al-Barakat arranges the types of definitions in an ascending order and places at the top of this order the hadd because it is the path of intrinsic knowledge, then after it the rasm because it is the path of accidental knowledge, and at the end of this order the tamthil because it does not yield either intrinsic or accidental knowledge. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi says: 'The benefit of definition by tamthil is that it is brought following the defining expressions, namely hudud and rusum, so it makes understood their content, not completing their concept, by familiarizing the mind with what is foreign from their expressions and bringing closer to it the distant implications and gathering for it the scattered meanings. It is very useful in education for bringing close to learners and lightening the load for teachers, but strong minds do not need it. The best of the expressions are hudud because they yield complete intrinsic knowledge, inferior to them are rusum because they yield accidental knowledge or mixed with accidental because they complete the incomplete intrinsic with the accidental taken from accidents and concomitants, and much less than them are tamthilat because they do not yield accidental or intrinsic knowledge, but are only for facilitating learning.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi's unique classification of definitions. He divides defining expressions into three: hudud (definitions proper), rusum (descriptions), and tamthilat (analogies). The first two are traditional (Aristotelian and Galenic), but tamthil is his addition. He defines tamthil as defining a thing by its likenesses or the universal by its particular instances. Abu al-Barakat then arranges these hierarchically: hudud are highest as they give essential (dhati) knowledge, rusum are lower as they give accidental knowledge, and tamthilat are lowest as they give neither essential nor accidental knowledge but merely facilitate understanding. He then quotes himself to explain the utility of tamthil: it makes the content of hudud and rusum comprehensible by familiarizing the mind with unfamiliar terms and gathering scattered meanings. It is useful for teaching but not needed by strong intellects. This hierarchical framework reflects a gradation of epistemological value.

الحدود
الرسوم

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Barakat ranks definitions by their epistemological value: hudud (essential) > rusum (accidental) > tamthilat (neither).

Abu al-Barakat's Threefold Classification of Definition
Author discusses post-Aristotelian division and Abu al-Barakat's threefold classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hadd, rasm, and tamthil and evaluate their epistemological ranking.

Core Idea
Definitions in Islamic logic are ranked: hadd (essence) > rasm (accidents) > tamthil (analogy).

In this passage, the author presents two classification systems for definitions. The first divides hadd and rasm into complete and incomplete. The second, from Abu al-Barakat, introduces a third type: tamthil. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for assessing the epistemic value of definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classification systems
Read the passage and note that it first mentions a post-Aristotelian division of hadd and rasm into complete and incomplete, then turns to Abu al-Barakat's threefold division of hadd, rasm, and tamthil.
The first division is not elaborated; it is just stated.
2
Extract the definitions of hadd, rasm, and tamthil
From the passage, identify how each type is defined: hadd gives essential knowledge through genus and differentia; rasm gives accidental knowledge through properties; tamthil defines by similars or by citing particular instances of a universal, without giving essential or accidental knowledge.
Abu al-Barakat explicitly defines tamthil in the quotation.
3
Analyze the hierarchical ranking
Notice that Abu al-Barakat places hadd at the top for providing complete essential knowledge, rasm in the middle for accidental knowledge, and tamthil at the bottom for merely facilitating understanding without adding new knowledge.
The ranking is based on the type of knowledge (essential/accidental/none).
4
Apply the ranking to a real Islamic example
Consider a definition of 'salat' in fiqh. A hadd would state its essence (e.g., specific words and actions with conditions). A rasm would describe its effects (e.g., prevents immorality). A tamthil would define it by likening it to other acts of worship.
This shows how the classification applies in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيماً آخر هو : تقسيم كل من الحد والرسم إلى تام وناقص . وهذا التقسيم لا يوجد عند أرسطو ، ومن المرجح أن يكون الإسلاميون أخذوه من شراح الإسكندرية . وينزع أبو البركات البغدادي في تقسيم التعريف منزعاً آخر ، فيقسم الأقاويل المعرفة إلى ثلاثة أقسام : الحدود والرسوم والتمثيلات . أما التعريف بالحد فهو عنصر أرسططاليسي ، والتعريف بالرسم عنصر جاليني وقد عرضناهما من قبل . يبقى إذن التعريف بالتمثيل وهو العنصر الجديد في هذا التقسيم . ويعرفه أبو البركات : بأنه « تعريف الشيء بنظائره وأشباهه والكلي المعقول بجزئياته وأشخاصه » وينظم أبو البركات أنواع التعاريف في نظام تصاعدي ويضع في قمة هذا النظام الحد لأنه طريق المعرفة الذاتية ، ثم يليه الرسم لأنه طريق المعرفة العرضية ، وفي آخر هذا النظام التمثيل ، لأنه لا يفيد لا معرفة ذاتية ولا عرضية . يقول أبو البركات البغدادي : « وفائدة التعريف بالتمثيل هو أنه يورد تبع الأقاويل المعرفة وهي الحدود والرسوم فيكون مفهماً لمضمونها لا متمماً لمفهومها بايناسه الذهن بما غرب عن ألفاظها وتقريبه عليه بعيد مدلولاتها وجمعاً لها متفرق معانيها وهو كثير النفع في التعاليم لتقريبه على المتعلمين ، وتخفيفه عند المعلمين ولكن لا تحتاج إليه الأذهان القوية . وأفضل الأقاويل الحدود لأنها تفيد المعرفة الذاتية التامة ، وأنقص منها الرسوم لأنها تفيد المعرفة العرضية أو مشوبة بالعرضية لأنها تتم الذاتية الناقصة بالعرضية المأخوذة من الأعراض واللواحق ، وأقل منها كثيراً التمثيلات لأنها لا تفيد معرفة عرضية ولا ذاتية وإنما هي لتسهيل الأفادة »
Another division is the division of each of hadd and rasm into complete and incomplete. This division is not found in Aristotle, and it is likely that the Muslims took it from the Alexandrian commentators. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi takes another course in dividing definition, so he divides explanatory statements into three sections: hadds, rasms, and tamthils. As for definition by hadd, it is an Aristotelian element; definition by rasm is a Galenic element, and we have presented both before. So remains definition by tamthil, which is the new element in this division. Abu al-Barakat defines it: 'It is the definition of a thing by its similars and analogues, and the definition of the universal intelligible by its particulars and individuals.' Abu al-Barakat arranges the types of definitions in an ascending order and places at the top of this order hadd because it is the path of essential knowledge, then follows rasm because it is the path of accidental knowledge, and at the end of this order is tamthil, because it benefits neither essential nor accidental knowledge. Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi says: 'The benefit of definition by tamthil is that it is attached to explanatory statements, namely hadds and rasms, so it makes their content understood, not completing their concept, by familiarizing the mind with what is strange about their expressions and approximating the distant meanings and gathering their scattered significations. It is very useful in teaching for its approximation for learners and its facilitation for teachers, but strong minds do not need it. The best of the explanatory statements are hadds because they provide complete essential knowledge; less than them are rasms because they provide accidental knowledge or knowledge mixed with accidentality, since they complete the incomplete essentiality by the accidentality taken from accidents and properties; and far less than them are tamthils because they provide neither accidental nor essential knowledge, but are only for facilitation of understanding.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents two classifications of definitions. First, it mentions a post-Aristotelian division of both hadd and rasm into complete (tamm) and incomplete (naqis), likely taken from Alexandrian commentators. Then it contrasts Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi's own threefold classification: hadd (essential definition), rasm (accidental description), and tamthil (definition by analogy). The author explains that hadd is Aristotelian, rasm is Galenic, and tamthil is the novel element. Abu al-Barakat defines tamthil as defining a thing by its likes or by citing particular instances of a universal. He then ranks definitions hierarchically: hadd is highest because it gives intrinsic knowledge; rasm is lower because it gives accidental knowledge; tamthil is lowest because it gives neither, serving only to facilitate understanding. The quotation from Abu al-Barakat elaborates on the utility of tamthil in teaching, noting it is not needed by strong minds. All explanations stay within the source text.

Verbal vs Real Definition: A Later Classification
Author presents a later classification of definition into verbal (lafzi) and real (haqiqi) as per Sullam al-'Ulum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal, real, and nominal definitions according to the later classification and apply the epistemic condition to examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Later logicians split definition by whether it explains a word or depicts a form with known existence.

The classification of definition into verbal (lafzi) and real (haqiqi) is a later development in Islamic logic, not found in Aristotle or earlier works like Isaghuji. This lesson unpacks the criteria for each type, especially the subtle distinction between real and nominal (bi-hasab al-ism) definitions based on whether the existence of the form or its effect is known. Understanding this helps you evaluate definitions used in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Locate in the Arabic the terms 'ta'rif lafzi' (verbal definition) and 'ta'rif haqiqi' (real definition). The passage states that the second (explanation) is verbal and the first (depiction) is real.
The author contrasts them as 'tashwir' (depiction) and 'tafsir' (explanation).
2
Understand the subcategory of real definition
Real definition is further split based on knowledge of existence: if the form or its effect is known to exist externally, it is real (haqiqi); otherwise, it is nominal (bi-hasab al-ism). Find the Arabic: 'فإن علم وجودها فهو بحسب الحقيقة وإلا فحسب الإسم'.
Nominal definition still acquires a form but without existential commitment.
3
Contrast verbal and real definitions
Verbal definition brings to mind the meaning of the word (إحضار مدلول اللفظ). Real definition depicts a form not yet obtained (تحصيل صورة غير حاصلة). Note that verbal does not require acquiring a new form; real does.
Verbal definition is often a synonym or lexical explanation; real definition gives essential nature.
4
Apply the classification to an Islamic example
Take a definition from Usul al-Fiqh, e.g., 'salah' (prayer). Classify a lexical definition ('salah means supplication') as verbal, a hadd ('salah is specific actions and words') as real, and a definition used in debating divine attributes as nominal if existence is not assumed.
Practice with terms like 'wudu' or 'sawm' from fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر بعض المتأخرين تقسيماً آخر للتعريف لا نجده أيضاً في تراث أرسطو . يقول صاحب سلم بحر العلوم : « معرف الشيء ما يحمل عليه تصويراً أو تفسيراً ـ الثاني اللفظي والأول الحقيقي . ففيه تحصيل صورة غير حاصلة ، فان علم وجودها فهو بحسب الحقيقة وإلا فحسب الإسم ، فالتعريف إذن بحسب اللفظ هو إحضار مدلول اللفظ والتعريف بحسب الحقيقة هو تصوير أمر غير حاصل بحيث يوجد هذا الأمر أو معلوله في الخارج ، والتعريف بحسب الإسم هو تحصيل صورة غير حاصلة أيضاً بحيث لا يعلم وجود تلك الصورة ولا معلولها في الخارج . من أين استمد الاسلاميون التعريف بحسب الإسم ؟ من المرجح أن تكون الرواقية هي مأخذ الإسلاميين هنا :
And some later [scholars] mention another division of definition that we also do not find in the heritage of Aristotle. The author of Sullam Bahr al-'Ulum says: 'The identifier (mu'arraf) of a thing is what is predicated of it by way of depiction or explanation — the second is verbal and the first is real. In it is the acquisition of a form not yet obtained; if its existence is known, it is according to reality, otherwise it is according to name. So definition according to the word is the bringing to mind of the meaning of the word, and definition according to reality is the depiction of a matter not yet obtained such that this matter or its effect exists externally. And definition according to the name is also the acquisition of a form not yet obtained, but in such a way that neither the existence of that form nor its effect is known externally.' Where did the Muslims derive definition according to the name? It is likely that Stoicism is the source of the Muslims here.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents a later classification of definition into two types: verbal (lafzi) and real (haqiqi). The author quotes Sahib Sullam Bahr al-'Ulum, who states that a definition is something that is predicated of a thing for depiction or explanation. The 'depiction' corresponds to real definition, and 'explanation' corresponds to verbal definition. In real definition, one acquires a form (image) of something not previously obtained, and if the existence of that thing is known, it is real; otherwise it is nominal (bi-hasab al-ism). Verbal definition brings to mind the meaning of the word. Nominal definition (ta'rif bi-hasab al-ism) also acquires a form but without knowing its existence or effect. The author then speculates that the Muslims derived nominal definition from Stoic logic.

Key Terms
تعريف لفظي
تعريف حقيقي
تعريف بحسب الإسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Real definition does not require any form.
B) Nominal definition assumes existence of the thing.
C) Real definition knows the existence of the portrayed form or its effect; nominal does not.
D) Both are the same.

Key Takeaway
The later division of definition into verbal, real, and nominal hinges on whether a form is acquired and whether its existence is known.

Nominal Definition and Its Stoic Roots
Author speculates on the Stoic origin of nominal definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the concept of nominal definition (ta'rif bi-hasab al-ism) to its likely Stoic origins and explain its utility in Islamic theological discourse.

Core Idea
Nominal definition is a Stoic inheritance that enriches Islamic logic's handling of unknown essences.

The passage ends with a historical question: where did the Muslims derive the concept of nominal definition? The author suggests Stoic logic as the likely source. This lesson explores that provenance and why nominal definition became a useful tool in kalam for discussing attributes of Allah without claiming full knowledge of the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the earlier absence
The passage states that the division into verbal and real is not found in Aristotle. Nominal definition amplifies this point—it is a novel addition. Note that earlier Islamic logic texts like Isaghuji do not mention it.
This suggests a later development influenced by non-Aristotelian sources.
2
Understand the epistemic condition of nominal definition
Nominal definition acquires a form without knowing whether that form or its effect exists externally. Contrast with real definition where existence is known. Find the Arabic: 'بحيث لا يعلم وجود تلك الصورة ولا معلولها في الخارج'.
This makes nominal definition suitable for discussing entities whose essence is unknown but whose concept is useful.
3
Connect to Stoic logic
The author speculates that the Muslims derived nominal definition from the Stoics. Stoic logic emphasized sign theory and definitions based on concepts rather than real essences. Consider how this might have entered Islamic thought through translations.
The Stoics distinguished between 'definition of a thing' and 'definition of a word', similar to the haqiqi/lafzi split.
4
Consider implications for Islamic theology
In kalam, when discussing Allah's attributes, scholars often use nominal definitions because the true essence of Allah is unknowable. For example, defining 'knowledge' as a divine attribute without claiming to know its reality. This epistemic humility aligns with nominal definition.
This tool allows theologians to talk about attributes while avoiding claims of comprehensive knowledge.

Source Text

The 'depiction' corresponds to real definition, and 'explanation' corresponds to verbal definition. In real definition, one acquires a form (image) of something not previously obtained, and if the existence of that thing is known, it is real; otherwise it is nominal (bi-hasab al-ism). Verbal definition brings to mind the meaning of the word. Nominal definition (ta'rif bi-hasab al-ism) also acquires a form but without knowing its existence or effect. The author then speculates that the Muslims derived nominal definition from Stoic logic.

Key Terms
تعريف بحسب الإسم
رواقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From Aristotle's Categories
B) From Stoic logic
C) From the Quran
D) From early Islamic grammar

Key Takeaway
Nominal definition, a unique addition to Islamic logic, likely has Stoic origins, reflecting the synthesis of Greek philosophical traditions.

Four Methods to Arrive at a Definition (Hadd)
Author outlines four methods to attain a definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four methods for attaining a definition (hadd) and explain why Aristotle rejected the first three.

Core Idea
The four methods to hadd — induction, division, demonstration, and Aristotle's own — each have strengths but Aristotle insisted only his method grasps quiddity.

This passage introduces the four scientific methods that Muslim logicians recognized for arriving at the hadd (definition) of something. You will learn each method, its origin, and why Aristotle — and later Muslim commentators — critiqued the first three as insufficient for capturing the essence (mahiyya) of a thing. Understanding these methods is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
List the four methods
Write down the four methods mentioned: induction (istiqra'), Platonic division (qisma), demonstration (burhan), and Aristotle's own method (not named).
These are the classical methods for arriving at a definition.
2
Identify the attributed originators
Note that induction is attributed to Socrates, division to Plato, demonstration to Hippocrates, and the fourth to Aristotle himself.
The attributions are historical, not necessarily accurate.
3
Determine Aristotle's stance
From the passage, why did Aristotle attack the first three? Because they do not lead to the idea of quiddity (mahiyya), the goal of analysis and synthesis.
Aristotle's own method is presumably the demonstrative syllogism that gives definitions.
4
Explore the Islamic reception
The passage says al-'Abdari presents the three methods, and al-Zarkashi notes disagreement: those who affirm composition of hadd considered division insufficient because it is purely analytical.
This shows how Islamic logicians engaged with Greek methodological debates.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider how a fuqaha might use induction to derive the definition of 'illah (legal cause) from multiple rulings, and why Aristotle's critique might apply: induction alone doesn't capture the essence of 'illah without additional demonstrative reasoning.
This connects the theoretical methods to actual Islamic legal theory.

And the fourth method is 'the way of the wise man' Aristotle himself. Commentary
This passage introduces the four methods used to attain a definition (hadd) in Islamic logic. The first is induction (istiqra'), attributed to Socrates. The second is Platonic division (qisma aflatuniyya). The third is demonstration (burhan), attributed to Hippocrates by the Muslims. The fourth is Aristotle's own method, which is not named here. The author notes that Aristotle criticized the first three for failing to grasp quiddity (mahiyya), his ultimate goal in analysis and synthesis. Despite Aristotle's own use of Platonic division, he strongly criticized Plato's theory. This critique is reflected in Islamic commentators like al-'Abdari. Al-Zarkashi reports that Muslims disagreed: those who believe the hadd is composed consider division insufficient because it is only analytical; whereas those who deny composition (perhaps the Ishraqiyyin or others) have a different view, though the text cuts off. The lesson focuses on listing the methods and their criticisms.
