Three Types of Definition: Real, Verbal, and Nominal
Author classifies definition into real, verbal, and nominal and traces nominal definition to Stoic influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the three types of definition (real, verbal, nominal) and identify which type is used in a given definition from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Definitions differ by whether we know the definiendum exists in reality.

This passage classifies definitions into three categories based on the epistemological status of the definiendum. The author distinguishes between real definition (when the existence of the thing or its effect is known), verbal definition (explaining the meaning of a word), and nominal definition (forming a concept without existential knowledge). He also suggests a historical link to Stoic logic. Understanding these distinctions helps you evaluate definitions in any Islamic science, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of classification
The passage distinguishes definitions based on whether the existence of the defined thing (or its effect) is known in the external world. If known, it is real (haqiqi); if not known, it is nominal (ismi). Verbal (lafzi) is separate—it only explains the word.
This is a key epistemological criterion.
2
Differentiate lafzi from ismi
Verbal definition (lafzi) brings to mind the meaning of a word, while nominal definition (ismi) forms a concept of a thing whose existence is not known. In later texts, they are sometimes conflated, but here they are distinct.
Remember: lafzi = about the word; ismi = about the thing but existence unknown.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'salah' (prayer). A real definition would depict its essence as known from revelation and action. A verbal definition would simply explain the word 'salah' means 'supplication'. A nominal definition might form a concept of a hypothetical act without asserting its actual performance.
Practice classifying definitions from fiqh or usul texts.
4
Trace the historical influence
The author suggests that the concept of nominal definition came from Stoic logic. This shows how Islamic logicians engaged with earlier traditions. Note that this is a historical claim, not a logical one.
The logical tool itself is independent of its origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تصويراً أو تفسيراً ـ الثاني اللفظي والأول الحقيقي . ففيه تحصيل صورة غير حاصلة ، فان علم وجودها فهو بحسب الحقيقة وإلا فحسب الإسم ، فالتعريف إذن بحسب اللفظ هو إحضار مدلول اللفظ والتعريف بحسب الحقيقة هو تصوير أمر غير حاصل بحيث يوجد هذا الأمر أو معلوله في الخارج ، والتعريف بحسب الإسم هو تحصيل صورة غير حاصلة أيضاً بحيث لا يعلم وجود تلك الصورة ولا معلولها في الخارج . من أين استمد الاسلاميون التعريف بحسب الإسم ؟ من المرجح أن تكون الرواقية هي مأخذ الإسلاميين هنا :
As a depiction or interpretation – the second is verbal and the first is real. For in it (the definition) is the acquisition of a form not obtained; if its existence is known, then it is according to reality, otherwise it is merely nominal. So definition according to verbal (expression) is bringing to mind the meaning of the word, and definition according to reality is depicting an unattained matter such that this matter or its effect exists in the external world, and definition according to name is the acquisition of a form also not obtained, such that the existence of that form or its effect is not known in the external world. From where did the Muslims derive definition according to name? It is probable that Stoicism is the source of the Muslims here:
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage defines three types of definition: real (haqiqi), verbal (lafzi), and nominal (ismi). The author begins by linking to the previous discussion (the first is real, the second is verbal). He explains that definition aims to acquire a form (image of the thing). If we know the existence of that form (i.e., it is actually existent), then the definition is haqiqi (real). If we do not know its existence, it is ismi (nominal). Then he clarifies each: al-ta'rif bi-hasb al-lafz is explaining the meaning of a word; al-ta'rif bi-hasb al-haqiqa is depicting something whose essence or effect exists in the outside world; al-ta'rif bi-hasb al-ism is forming a concept without knowing whether the thing or its effect exists. The author then raises the historical question of the source of nominal definition in Islamic logic, suggesting Stoic influence.

Four Methods for Attaining the Definition in Islamic Logic
Author lists four methods for attaining definition (induction, division, demonstration, Aristotle's method) and notes Aristotle's critique of the first three.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four methods for attaining the definition and explain Aristotle's critique of the first three.

Core Idea
Definition-seeking methods: induction, division, demonstration – all rejected by Aristotle for failing to capture essence.

This lesson explores the classical Islamic account of the methods used to arrive at a definition (hadd). The Islamic logicians inherited a tradition that recognized four main paths: induction (istiqra'), Platonic division (qisma), demonstration (burhan), and the unique method of Aristotle himself. The passage also records Aristotle's sharp critique of the first three, which echoes in later commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four methods
Locate the four methods in the Arabic text. Note their names and attributed origins: induction (Socrates), division (Plato), demonstration (Hippocrates), and Aristotle's own method.
The fourth method is called 'madhhab al-hakim' (the way of the sage).
2
Describe each method briefly
For each method, summarize its core procedure: induction from particulars to a general rule, division of a genus into species, demonstration via syllogistic proof, and Aristotle's method (not defined here).
3
Understand Aristotle's critique
Identify the common deficiency Aristotle found in the first three methods: they do not lead to the essence (mahiyya), which is the goal of definition.
Essence is the quiddity that makes a thing what it is.
4
Note the irony in Aristotle's attack
The text mentions that Aristotle frequently used Platonic division in his own analyses, yet he severely attacked Plato's theory. Why might he still use it?
This suggests a pragmatic use despite theoretical rejection.
5
Connect to Islamic commentators
The passage states that Aristotle's attack echoes among Islamic commentators. How did later Islamic logicians engage with this critique?
Consider the role of al-Farabi, Ibn Sina, and others in preserving and refining these ideas.

And it is well known that Aristotle attacked the first three methods, considering them incapable of reaching the definition because they do not lead to the idea of quiddity (essence), which is the goal of analysis and synthesis according to him. And although Aristotle frequently uses Platonic division in analysis itself, he did not refrain from severely attacking Plato's theory. This attack finds its echo among the Islamic commentators.

Commentary

Interestingly, despite his critique, Aristotle himself often used Platonic division in his own analyses, yet he still launched a severe attack on Plato's theory. This indicates a nuanced relationship. The author remarks that this attack is reflected in the works of Islamic logicians.

Does Division Yield Definition? The Islamic Debate
Author presents conflicting views among Islamic logicians on whether division yields the definition, including Imam al-Haramayn's equivalence thesis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conflicting positions among Islamic logicians on whether division (taqsim) can produce a real definition (hadd) and evaluate the grounds of each position.

Core Idea
Division is either rejected as mere analysis or embraced as equivalent to definition itself.

Islamic logicians did not unanimously accept division as a path to definition. Some saw it as purely analytical, while others, including Imam al-Haramayn, considered it a full-fledged method. This lesson maps the conflicting stances, drawing on al-Zarkashi, later commentators, and al-Juwayni's radical equivalence thesis. Understanding this debate reveals the pluralism within Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two camps on composition of definition
The passage divides logicians into those who affirm that definition is composed of genus and differentia (tarkib) and those who deny composition. Find the sentence that introduces this split.
Look for 'القائلون بتركيب الحد' and 'من منع التركيب في الحد'.
2
Extract the two later opinions on division
Later commentators gave two views: one holds division yields definition because it aims to establish one of two attributes, the other distinguishes between division within the definition (useless) and as an external tool (useful). Locate these.
The second opinion uses a conditional: 'ان كان في نفس الحد... ان كان خارجاً عنه'.
3
Analyze Imam al-Haramayn's equivalence thesis
Al-Juwayni states that division is a mental operation equivalent to definition: both lead to reality. Find the phrase 'عملية عقلية توازي الحد تماماً' and note how this differs from the preceding views.
This is the strongest endorsement of division in the passage.
4
Map the broader eclecticism
The author concludes that Islamic logicians combined Peripatetic, Stoic, Socratic, and Platonic elements. Identify the three sources mentioned (Peripatetics, Stoics, Socratic induction, Platonic division) and the author's claim that Muslims deviated from pure Aristotelianism.
The phrase 'ابتعدوا عن الفكر الأرسططاليسي ابتعاداً تاماً' summarizes this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعبدري يعرض الطرق الثلاثة الموصلة الى الحد . ويذكر الزركشي أن المسلمين وقعوا أمام مسألة الطرق الموصلة للحد مواقف متعارضة ـ فالقائلون بتركيب الحد اعتبروا القسمة لا توصل إلى الحد لأن القسمة عملية تحليلية فحسب . وأما من منع التركيب في الحد ، فأجاز التوصل إليه بالقسمة(١) . وللمتأخرين من شراح المنطق الإسلاميين رأيان في أن التقسيم يفيد الحد أم لا يفيده . أما الرأي الأول فهو أن التقسيم يؤدي إلى التوصل للحد لأن الغاية من التقسيم التوصل إلى اثبات احدى الصفتين لا مجرد التردد بينهما . الرأي الثاني أن التقسيم ان كان في نفس الحد لا يفيده ، أما إن كان خارجاً عنه ـ أي يستخدم كأداة له فقط على ألا يكون الجزء الجوهري فيه ـ فانه يصل بنا إلى الحد . بل إن الأمر لم يقف عند حد هذا ، بل إننا نرى مفكراً ممتازاً هو إمام الحرمين يعتبر التقسيم عملية عقلية توازي الحد تماماً . فإذا كنا نتوصل بالحد الى درك الحقيقة ، فاننا نتوصل إلى ادراكها أيضاً بواسطة التقسيم . ويحاول صاحب التعليقات على البصائر النصيرية أن يثبت إمكان التوصل إلى الحد بواسطة الإستقراء والقسمة والبرهان(٢) . ينتهي هنا ما أردنا توضيحه من مبحث التعريف عند الإسلاميين ، ولا نستطيع أن نقول : ان هذا المبحث كان جميعه لدى الشراح الإسلاميين أرسططاليسياً ، بل انقسم الإسلاميون فيه إلى مشائين ورواقيين . وتخللت هذه المباحث أيضاً عناصر سقراطية وأفلاطونية ، استمدها الإسلاميون من الأورجانون نفسه . فقد عرض أرسطو للإستقراء السقراطي كما عرض للقسمة الأفلاطونية . ولم يأخذ الإسلاميون من المعلم الأول الجانب السالب فقط ، أي نقده لهذه الطرق ، ولكن أخذوا أيضاً بالجانب الموجب لتلك الطرق . فأخذوا بها واعتروها قوانين عقلية تصل بها إلى الحد ، فابتعدوا عن الفكر الأرسططاليسي ابتعاداً تاماً ، وهذا ما أردنا توضيحه من هذا العرض الموجز .
Al-ʿAbdari presents the three methods that lead to the definition. And al-Zarkashi mentions that the Muslims held conflicting positions on the question of the methods leading to the definition: those who assert composition in the definition consider that division does not lead to the definition because division is merely an analytical operation; but those who deny composition in the definition permit arriving at it through division. And for the later commentators of Islamic logic, there are two opinions on whether division yields the definition or not. The first opinion is that division does lead to the definition because the purpose of division is to establish one of two attributes, not merely to vacillate between them. The second opinion is that if division is within the definition itself, it does not yield it; but if it is external to it—i.e., used only as a tool provided it is not an essential part—then it leads us to the definition. Indeed, the matter did not stop at this, but we see an excellent thinker, Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), considering division to be a mental operation equivalent to definition entirely. For if we arrive at reality through definition, we also arrive at its comprehension through division. And the author of al-Ta'liqat on al-Basa'ir al-Nasiriyya attempts to prove the possibility of arriving at the definition through induction, division, and demonstration. Here ends what we intended to clarify concerning the topic of definition among the Muslims. And we cannot say that this whole topic among the Islamic commentators is Aristotelian; rather, the Muslims split in it into Peripatetics and Stoics. And these discussions also contained Socratic and Platonic elements, which the Muslims derived from the Organon itself. For Aristotle presented Socratic induction as well as Platonic division. And the Muslims took from the First Teacher not only the negative aspect, i.e., his critique of these methods, but also the positive aspect of those methods. So they adopted them and considered them rational laws that lead to the definition, thus deviating completely from Aristotelian thought. And this is what we wanted to clarify in this brief presentation.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the debate among Islamic logicians regarding the method of division (qisma/tagsim) as a means to reach a real definition (hadd). It begins by mentioning al-ʿAbdari's presentation of the three methods and al-Zarkashi's report of conflicting stances: those who hold that definition is composed of genus and differentia contend that division, being merely analytical, cannot produce a definition; whereas those who reject composition in definition allow it. Then it introduces two opinions among later commentators: the first holds that division is useful because its goal is to prove one of two attributes, not mere hesitation; the second distinguishes between division within the definition (which is useless) and division as an external tool (which is useful). The passage then elevates the discussion by citing Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), who equates division with definition as a mental operation leading to true knowledge. The author of 'al-Ta'liqat' on 'al-Basa'ir al-Nasiriyya' is noted as trying to prove that induction, division, and demonstration all can lead to definition. Finally, the author concludes that this topic in Islamic logic is not purely Aristotelian: the Muslims incorporated Stoic, Socratic, and Platonic elements, taking from Aristotle both the negative critiques and the positive aspects of those methods, thus departing from pure Aristotelianism.

التقسيم
التركيب
إمام الحرمين
المشائين
الرواقيين

Checkpoint
What was the position of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) on division (taqsim) in relation to definition?
A) He considered division useless for reaching the definition.
B) He considered division equivalent to definition as a mental operation.
C) He considered division useful only as an external tool.
D) He rejected both division and definition.

Key Takeaway
Islamic logicians held conflicting views on division: from rejection to equivalence, reflecting a pluralistic heritage beyond pure Aristotelianism.

Two Views on Division Yielding Definition
Author presents two views on division as a method for definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two views on whether division yields definition and identify how Islamic logicians departed from Aristotelian orthodoxy.

Core Idea
Division is not just classification—it can be a tool for definition, and Islamic logicians went beyond Aristotle.

This passage presents two contrasting opinions among later Islamic logicians about whether the logical operation of division (taqsim) can produce a definition (hadd). It also highlights how Islamic logicians creatively integrated Socratic, Platonic, and Stoic elements, moving beyond strict Aristotelianism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on division
The first view holds that division yields definition because its purpose is to affirm one of two attributes, not merely hesitate. The second view distinguishes division within the definition (does not yield) from division as an external tool (yields).
The external tool condition: division is used only as an instrument, not as an essential part of the definition.
2
Note Imam al-Haramayn's radical position
He considers division completely equivalent to definition as a mental process for grasping reality. This elevates division to a primary epistemic method.
Imam al-Haramayn is known in usul al-fiqh; his view here reflects the deep integration of logical methods into Islamic sciences.
3
Recognize the broader historical claim
Islamic logicians did not merely replicate Aristotle's critique of division and induction; they positively adopted these methods from Socratic, Platonic, and Stoic sources, departing from pure Aristotelian thought.
This shows the intellectual independence and eclecticism of the Islamic logical tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الحد، فأجاز التوصل إليه بالقسمة(١) وللمتأخرين من شراح المنطق الإسلاميين رأيان في أن التقسيم يفيد الحد أم لا يفيده . أما الرأي الأول فهو أن التقسيم يؤدي إلى التوصل للحد لأن الغاية من التقسيم التوصل إلى اثبات احدى الصفتين لا مجرد التردد بينهما . الرأي الثاني أن التقسيم ان كان في نفس الحد لا يفيده ، أما إن كان خارجاً عنه ـ أي يستخدم كأداة له فقط على ألا يكون الجزء الجوهري فيه ـ فانه يصل بنا إلى الحد . بل إن الأمر لم يقف عند حد هذا ، بل إننا نرى مفكراً ممتازاً هو إمام الحرمين يعتبر التقسيم عملية عقلية توازي الحد تماماً . فإذا كنا نتوصل بالحد الى درك الحقيقة ، فاننا نتوصل إلى ادراكها أيضاً بواسطة التقسيم . ويحاول صاحب التعليقات على البصائر النصيرية أن يثبت إمكان التوصل إلى الحد بواسطة الإستقراء والقسمة والبرهان(٢) . ينتهي هنا ما أردنا توضيحه من مبحث التعريف عند الإسلاميين ، ولا نستطيع أن نقول : ان هذا المبحث كان جميعه لدى الشراح الإسلاميين أرسططاليسياً ، بل انقسم الإسلاميون فيه إلى مشائين ورواقيين . وتخللت هذه المباحث أيضاً عناصر سقراطية وأفلاطونية ، استمدها الإسلاميون من الأورجانون نفسه . فقد عرض أرسطو للإستقراء السقراطي كما عرض للقسمة الأفلاطونية . ولم يأخذ الإسلاميون من المعلم الأول الجانب السالب فقط ، أي نقده لهذه الطرق ، ولكن أخذوا أيضاً بالجانب الموجب لتلك الطرق . فأخذوا بها واعتبروها قوانين عقلية تصل بها إلى الحد ، فابتعدوا عن الفكر الأرسططاليسي ابتعاداً تاماً ، وهذا ما أردنا توضيحه من هذا العرض الموجز .
Concerning definition, so he allowed arriving at it through division. The later commentators of Islamic logic have two opinions on whether division yields definition or not. As for the first opinion, it is that division leads to arriving at definition because the goal of division is to arrive at affirming one of two attributes, not merely hesitation between them. The second opinion: if division is within the definition itself it does not yield it; but if it is outside it—i.e., used only as a tool for it on condition that it is not an essential part—then it brings us to the definition. Indeed, the matter did not stop at this limit; rather we see an excellent thinker, Imam al-Haramayn, considers division a mental process equivalent to definition entirely. So if we arrive at grasping reality through definition, we also arrive at perceiving it through division. The author of the Ta'liqat 'ala al-Basa'ir al-Nasiriyya attempts to prove the possibility of arriving at definition through induction, division, and demonstration. This ends what we wanted to clarify of the topic of definition among the Islamic logicians. We cannot say that this entire topic among the Islamic commentators was Aristotelian; rather the Islamic logicians divided into Peripatetics and Stoics. These discussions also included Socratic and Platonic elements, which the Islamic logicians derived from the Organon itself. For Aristotle presented Socratic induction as he presented Platonic division. The Islamic logicians did not take from the First Teacher only the negative aspect, i.e., his critique of these methods, but also took the positive aspect of those methods. So they adopted them and considered them rational laws that lead to definition, thereby completely departing from Aristotelian thought, and this is what we intended to clarify in this brief exposition.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage discusses the utility of division (taqsim) in arriving at definition (hadd) within Islamic logic. It opens by stating that some allowed reaching definition through division, then presents two later commentators' views. The first view holds that division is useful because its purpose is to affirm one of two attributes, not just to vacillate. The second view distinguishes between division internal to the definition (which does not yield it) and external division used as a mere tool (which does yield it). The author then cites Imam al-Haramayn, who elevates division to a mental process equivalent to definition: just as definition grasps reality, so does division. Additionally, the author of Ta'liqat 'ala al-Basa'ir al-Nasiriyya attempts to prove that definition can be reached through induction, division, and demonstration. The passage concludes that Islamic logicians were not purely Aristotelian in this topic; they incorporated Peripatetic, Stoic, Socratic, and Platonic elements from the Organon, taking both Aristotle's critique and the positive methods themselves, thus departing from pure Aristotelianism.

Key Terms
التقسيم
الحد
الإستقراء
البرهان

Checkpoint

D) Division never yields definition

Key Takeaway
Division (taqsim) can serve as a constructive method for definition when used properly, and Islamic logicians adopted it positively, departing from Aristotle's critique.

Two Schools of Usul al-Fiqh: Jurists vs Theologians
Author divides usul scholars into two groups and describes their methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the jurist-usuliyyun from the theologian-usuliyyun based on their method, key figures, and relationship to Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Usul al-fiqh scholars split into jurists blending fiqh and usul, and theologians using pure reason.

This passage introduces a fundamental division in the history of usul al-fiqh: the jurist-usuliyyun (fuqaha') and the theologian-usuliyyun (mutakallimun). Understanding this division is crucial for appreciating why different usul works have different styles and epistemologies. The passage also claims that the jurist-usuliyyun's works are free from Aristotelian logic, a significant observation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups
Find the names of the two groups in the Arabic: 'الأصوليين الفقهاء' and 'الأصوليين المتكلمين'. Note the author's basis for this division: the influence of theologians starting to write on usul.
2
Describe the jurist-usuliyyun method
Look for key phrases: blended usul with fiqh, many subsidiary issues, built on juristic points. Who are their representatives? Hanafi jurists like al-Dabusi, al-Bazdawi, etc.
3
Describe the theologian-usuliyyun method
Look for: rational attempt, abstracting general rules, relying on rational inference and theoretical proof. They include both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili.
4
Check the role of Aristotelian logic
The passage explicitly says the jurist-usuliyyun works are free from Aristotelian logic. What about the theologians? The passage doesn't say they used it, but it mentions their rational methods.
5
Connect to later development
Note that Ibn al-Sa'ati combined both methods, showing later synthesis. This indicates the two streams were not entirely separate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما التطور الحقيقي في علم أصول الفقه ـ فقد حدث تحت تأثير حركة فكرية جديدة هي بدء المتكلمين في التصنيف فيه(١). وعلى هذا الأساس يمكننا تقسيم علماء الأصول إلى قسمين : الأصوليين الفقهاء والأصوليين المتكلمين . أما الأول فقد امتزجت في كتاباتهم الأصول بالفقه وكثر تفريع المسائل الجزئية وذكر الأمثلة والشواهد ـ وبنيت المسائل الكلية العامة على النكت الفقهية ـ ويمثل هذا القسم فقهاء الحنفية ، ومن أئمتهم المتقدمين الدبوسي ( ٤٣٠ هـ ) وقد كتب في القياس بأوسع ممن كان قبله وتمم مباحثه وشرائطه ـ وكان له أكبر الأثر في المعاونة على إكمال المنهج الفقهي(٢) ـ وكتابه هو تأسيس النظر . ثم كتب البزدوي كتاباً مطولاً ـ كشف الأسرار ـ ثم جمع ابن الساعاتي(٣) ( ٦١٤ هـ ) بين طريقة المتكلمين والفقهاء مستنداً إلى كتابي الأحكام للآمدي وكشف الأسرار للبزدوي في كتاب سماه « بدائع النظام » ثم كتب الإمام الشاطبي ( ٧٩٠ هـ ) كتاب الموافقات ، وسار أيضاً على هذا النهج شهاب الدين القرافي ( ٦٨٤ هـ ) وله كتاب الذخيرة ، وكتاب أنوار البروق في نواء الفروق ، ثم كتب السبكي كتابه المشهور « جمع الجوامع » وقد اقتصر ابن السبكي في هذا الكتاب على ذكر مسائل الأصول مجردة من الأدلة والشواهد ، وقد شرح هذا الكتاب شروحاً عدة كان أهمها شرح المحلي(٤) ( ٧٦١ هـ ٨٦٤ م ) وشرح الزركشي ( ٧٩٤ هـ ) . وهذه الكتب كلها بين أيدينا وقد خلت من أي تأثير بالمنطق الأرسططاليسي . أما القسم الثاني وهم المتكلمون أشاعرة كانوا أو معتزلة(٥) . وهنا نرى محاولة عقلية بحتة تقوم على تجريد القواعد العامة من المسائل الفقهية وتستند في هذا إلى الاستدلال العقلي والبرهنة النظرية(٦) . فاستخدمت طرق البحث
As for the real development in the science of usul al-fiqh, it occurred under the influence of a new intellectual movement, namely the beginning of the theologians' classification in it(1). On this basis we can divide the scholars of usul into two groups: the jurist-usuliyyun and the theologian-usuliyyun. As for the first group, in their writings usul blended with fiqh, there was much branching of subsidiary issues and mention of examples and evidences, and universal general issues were built on juristic points. This group is represented by the Hanafi jurists, and among their early imams is al-Dabusi (d. 430 AH) who wrote about qiyas more extensively than anyone before him, completed its topics and conditions, and had the greatest impact in helping complete the jurisprudential method(2). His book is Ta'sis al-Nazar. Then al-Bazdawi wrote an extensive book, Kashf al-Asrar. Then Ibn al-Sa'ati(3) (d. 614 AH) combined the method of the theologians and the jurists, relying on the two books al-Ahkam by al-Amidi and Kashf al-Asrar by al-Bazdawi, in a book he called Bada'i' al-Nizam. Then Imam al-Shatibi (d. 790 AH) wrote al-Muwafaqat. Shihab al-Din al-Qarafi (d. 684 AH) also followed this path; he has al-Dhakhira and Anwar al-Buruq fi Anwa' al-Furuq. Then al-Subki wrote his famous book Jam' al-Jawami'. Ibn al-Subki restricted this book to mentioning usul issues stripped of evidences and illustrations. This book received several commentaries, the most important being the commentary by al-Mahalli(4) (761 AH / 864 CE) and that by al-Zarkashi (d. 794 AH). All these books are in our hands and are free from any influence of Aristotelian logic. As for the second group, they are the theologians, whether Ash'ari or Mu'tazili(5). Here we see a purely rational attempt based on abstracting general rules from fiqh issues and relying on rational inference and theoretical proof(6). Thus they used research methods
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage divides usul al-fiqh scholars into two distinct groups. The author first states that the real development in usul came from a new intellectual movement: theologians beginning to write on the subject. Then he presents the division. The first group, the jurist-usuliyyun, integrated usul with fiqh, focusing on subsidiary issues and examples. They are represented by Hanafi scholars like al-Dabusi, who wrote extensively on qiyas; al-Bazdawi; and later figures like Ibn al-Sa'ati, who combined both methods; al-Shatibi; al-Qarafi; and al-Subki. The author notes that all these works are free from Aristotelian logic. The second group consists of theologians, both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili, who pursued a purely rational approach, abstracting general principles from fiqh issues and relying on rational proof. The passage ends mid-sentence with 'thus they used research methods', indicating a continuation.

Key Terms
الأصوليين الفقهاء
الأصوليين المتكلمين
القياس
المنطق الأرسططاليسي

Checkpoint

A) The theologian-usuliyyun
B) The jurist-usuliyyun
C) Both groups equally
D) Neither group

Key Takeaway
Usul al-fiqh developed through two distinct schools: the jurist-usuliyyun, who blended usul with fiqh and avoided Greek logic, and the theologian-usuliyyun, who pursued rational abstraction.

Key Figures of the Jurist-Usuliyyun and Their Works
Author lists jurist-usuliyyun scholars and their works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the major jurist-usuliyyun scholars and their key works mentioned in the passage, and explain their contributions.

Core Idea
Al-Dabusi, al-Bazdawi, Ibn al-Sa'ati, al-Shatibi, al-Qarafi, al-Subki: a chain of Hanafi usul works that remained logic-free.

The passage provides a chronology of the jurist-usuliyyun school, starting from al-Dabusi's Ta'sis al-Nazar to al-Subki's Jam' al-Jawami'. Knowing these names and their works helps you navigate the primary sources of Hanafi usul. This lesson focuses on memorizing the key authors and their contributions.

Guided Path
1
Find the first major figure
Who is mentioned as the earliest? Al-Dabusi (d. 430 AH), author of Ta'sis al-Nazar, expanded on qiyas.
2
Trace the development
Next is al-Bazdawi with Kashf al-Asrar. Then Ibn al-Sa'ati who combined methods in Bada'i' al-Nizam.
3
Note the later figures
Al-Shatibi (al-Muwafaqat), al-Qarafi (al-Dhakhira, Anwar al-Buruq), al-Subki (Jam' al-Jawami'), and its commentators al-Mahalli and al-Zarkashi.
4
Observe the pattern
All these works are described as free from Aristotelian logic. Why might the author emphasize this? It highlights a distinct Hanafi method.

The Nature and Structure of Usuli Logic
Author defines the logic of the Usuliyyun and outlines its two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of usuli logic and distinguish it from Aristotelian logic.

Core Idea
Usuli logic is a practical, metaphysics-free methodology, not a theoretical science.

In this opening passage, the author clarifies what usuli logic is and why it matters. After establishing that the usuliyyun (scholars of usul al-fiqh and kalam) rejected Aristotelian logic, he now defines the alternative they developed. Understanding this definition is the foundation for appreciating the two-part structure—definition and inferences—that follows.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The passage defines 'usuli logic' (al-mantiq al-uṣūlī). Note that this is a technical term specific to the author's analysis, not a standard category in classical Islamic logic.
2
Extract the genus
The author places usuli logic under the genus 'method of scientific research' (manhaj min manāhij al-baḥth al-ʿilmī). This tells us that usuli logic is a practical tool, not a theoretical science like Aristotelian logic.
3
Identify the differentia
The key differentia is that usuli logic is free from metaphysical discussions (khalī min mabāḥith al-mītāfīzīqā). This freedom makes it a 'practical' (pragmatique) logic suited to human needs, as opposed to Aristotelian logic's claim to be the science of necessary thought identical with being.
4
Contrast with Aristotelian logic
The author explicitly contrasts usuli logic with Aristotelian logic, which he characterizes as metaphysical and theoretical. This contrast highlights why the usuliyyun felt the need for a different logic.
5
Note the division into two topics
Usuli logic is divided into two parts: the topic of definition (mabḥath al-ḥadd) and the topic of inferences (mabḥath al-istidlālāt). This structure will guide the rest of the chapter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفَصْلُ الثَّالِثُ
منطق الأصوليين مبحث الحد الأصولي
كانت النتيجة التي وصلنا إليها في الفصلين السابقين أن الأصوليين علماء أصول الفقه وعلماء أصول الدين - المتكلمين - لم يقبلوا المنطق الأرسططاليسي وأنهم وضعوا منطقاً يخالف هذا المنطق . وقلنا إن علماء أصول الفقه وعلماء أصول الدين استخدموا منهجاً للبحث أو منطقاً واحداً . ولكن ما هو هذا المنطق الأصولي الذي استخدمه الأصوليون ؟ هذا المنطق الأصولي في الحقيقة هو منهج من مناهج البحث العلمي الذي وضعه الأصوليون لكي يسيروا عليه في أبحاثهم وكانت أميز صفاته أنه يخلو من مباحث الميتافيزيقا - أو من الصفة الميتافيزيقية تلك الصفة التي جعلت المنطق الارسططاليسي كأنه علم الفكر الضروري من حيث هو متطابق مع الموجود La science de la pensée necessaire en tant qu'identique avec l'être أو كما يدعيه الهجليون علم الفكرة المجردة(١) La science de l'idée pure وخلوه من الناحية الميتافيزيقية جعله منطقاً عملياً أو بمعنى أدق منطقاً pragmatique يتفق مع الحاجة الإنسانية العملية . وينقسم هذا المنطق إلى مبحثين : المبحث الأول هو مبحث الحد . والمبحث الثاني هو مبحث الإستدلالات . وسنبحث في هذا الفصل مبحث الحد - كما عرفه الأصوليون - طبقاً لشذرات قليلة بقيت لنا عنه كتب المتأخرين .
The Third Chapter: The Logic of the Usuliyyun: The Topic of the Usuli Definition

The conclusion we reached in the previous two chapters is that the usuliyyun—scholars of usul al-fiqh and scholars of usul al-din (the theologians)—did not accept Aristotelian logic and they developed a logic that opposes this logic. And we said that scholars of usul al-fiqh and scholars of usul al-din used one method of inquiry or one logic. But what is this usuli logic that the usuliyyun used? This usuli logic is in reality one of the methods of scientific research that the usuliyyun devised in order to proceed in their investigations, and its most distinguished characteristic is that it is free from the discussions of metaphysics—or from the metaphysical quality, that quality which made Aristotelian logic seem like the science of necessary thought inasmuch as it is identical with being (La science de la pensée nécessaire en tant qu'identique avec l'être), or as the Hegelians call it, the science of pure idea (La science de l'idée pure). And its freedom from the metaphysical aspect made it a practical logic, or more precisely a pragmatique logic that agrees with human practical need. And this logic is divided into two topics: the first topic is the topic of definition, and the second topic is the topic of inferences. And we will discuss in this chapter the topic of definition—as defined by the usuliyyun—according to a few fragments that remain for us from the books of later scholars.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter. It summarizes the preceding conclusion: the usuliyyun (both legal theorists and theologians) rejected Aristotelian logic and created their own. The author then asks: what is this usuli logic? He defines it as a methodology of scientific research devised by the usuliyyun. Its key feature is being free from metaphysics, contrasting with Aristotelian logic which claims to be the science of necessary thought identical with being (citing French phrases to characterize it). This freedom makes it a practical, 'pragmatic' logic suited to human needs. The logic is then divided into two branches: the first is the topic of definition (hadd), and the second is the topic of inferences (istidlalat). The chapter will focus on the usuli conception of definition, based on surviving fragments.

Key Takeaway
Usuli logic is defined as a practical, metaphysics-free methodology of scientific research, divided into the topics of definition and inferences.

The Usuli Critique of Aristotelian Definition
Author presents the two objections against the Aristotelian definition and offers the usuli alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two usuli objections to the Aristotelian definition and explain their alternative definition.

Core Idea
The usuliyyun reject essence-revealing definitions; they define a thing by its intended description.

The Aristotelian tradition defined definition as that which reveals the essence of a thing. The usuliyyun, however, found this metaphysical goal unattainable and unnecessary for Islamic jurisprudence. In this passage, the author presents their two objections and offers a pragmatic alternative rooted in the intended description (al-waṣf al-maqṣūd).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Aristotelian definition being rejected
Find in the Arabic the three formulations: definition as knower of essence, as knower of self, as correct answer to 'what is it?'.
2
Extract the first objection
The definition may be mentioned without a question. This shows definition is not essentially tied to an interrogative context.
3
Extract the second objection
The definition does not reveal the essence because such knowledge is difficult or impossible. Note the word 'السكنة' (tranquility) – definition cannot provide certainty.
The second objection is more philosophical: metaphysical essence is unknowable.
4
Locate the usuli alternative
Find the sentence: 'But the definition of a thing is its meaning for which it deserves the intended description.' This shifts from metaphysical to operational.
5
Compare the two approaches
Aristotle aims at essence; usuliyyun aim at distinguishing a thing for a specific purpose (the intended description).
The intended description is context-dependent, e.g., in fiqh definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن الحد عند أرسطو ـ كما رأينا ـ هو المعرف للماهية أو للذات أو بأنه الجواب الصحيح في سؤال ما هو إذا أحاط بالمسؤول عنه » هذه التعاريف كلها ميتافيزيقية لم يقبلها الأصوليون وأوردوا عليها الإعتراضين الآتيين ـ أولهما : أن الحد قد يذكر من غير أن يكون هناك سؤال ـ ثانياً : أن الحد ليس معرفاً للماهية أو موصلاً للسكنة لعسر التوصل إلى هذا أو استحالته . لكن حد الشيء هو معناه الذي لأجله استحق الوصف المقصود(۱) . وهذا اتجاه في تعريف الحد يخالف الحد الأرسططاليسي . وسنوضح هذا توضيحاً أكثر .
The definition according to Aristotle—as we have seen—is that which makes known the essence or the self, or that it is the correct answer to the question 'what is it?' when it comprehends what is asked about. These definitions are all metaphysical; the usuliyyun did not accept them and raised the following two objections against them: first, that the definition may be mentioned without there being a question; second, that the definition is not a knower of the essence nor a conveyer of tranquility (sakina) due to the difficulty or impossibility of attaining this. But the definition of a thing is its meaning for which it deserves the intended description (1). And this is a direction in defining that opposes the Aristotelian definition. And we will explain this further.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents the usuli critique of the Aristotelian concept of definition. The author begins by recalling that Aristotle defined definition as something that makes known the essence (mahiyya) or the self (dhat), or as the correct answer to the question 'what is it?'. The usuliyyun reject these definitions as metaphysical and give two specific objections. First, a definition can be given without any question being asked—so linking it to an interrogative context is unnecessary. Second, and more fundamentally, a definition does not actually reveal the essence or convey certainty (sakina), because attaining such knowledge is extremely difficult or impossible. The author then states the usuli alternative: 'the definition of a thing is its meaning for which it deserves the intended description.' This is a pragmatic, distinction-based definition, not an essence-revealing one. The author promises further clarification. This passage thus sets up the contrast between the metaphysical Aristotelian approach and the practical usuli approach.

Key Terms
الحد
الماهية
الوصف المقصود

Checkpoint

A) It is too complex.
B) It does not convey tranquility because essence is hard to know.
C) It requires a question.
D) It is not based on syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The usuliyyun replace the metaphysical goal of defining essence with a pragmatic goal of capturing the meaning for which a thing deserves its intended description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Essential Properties vs. Mere Distinction: Two Purposes of Definition
Author contrasts Aristotelian and usuli purposes of definition, citing al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the Aristotelian purpose of definition (ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt) from the usuli purpose (mujarrad al-tamyīz) and explain how each affects the content of a definition.

Core Idea
The purpose of definition determines what counts as a good definition — essential enumeration or mere distinction.

What is the goal of defining something? Is it to list all its essential properties, or merely to set it apart from others? This passage presents two competing visions from Aristotelian logicians and usuli scholars. Understanding this difference is foundational to how definitions are constructed in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes
Read the first Arabic sentence: it asks whether definition aims at 'ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt' or 'mujarrad al-tamyīz'. Note these two keywords.
2
Extract each school's definition
For the Aristotelians, find the phrase 'al-qawl al-mufaṣṣil al-muʿarrif li-l-dhāt bi-māhiyyatihi'. For the usuliyyun, find 'al-qawl al-mufassir li-ism al-ḥadd wa-ṣifatihi'. Write them down.
These are the definitions of 'definition' itself according to each school.
3
Compare conditions
The Aristotelians require the description to be 'specific' (khāṣṣ) and refer to the reality of the defined. The usuliyyun require the description to 'specify and restrict' (yuṣaḥḥiḥuhu wa-yaḥṣuruhu). How are these conditions similar and different?
Both aim to exclude non-instances, but the Aristotelians demand deeper essential content.
4
Apply to a fiqh term
Imagine defining 'zakāt'. An Aristotelian definition would list essential properties like 'obligatory charity from specific wealth with conditions'. A usuli definition might simply say 'obligatory transfer of ownership for Allah' – enough to distinguish it from other transfers.
The usuli definition is shorter and more practical for legal distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن تعريف الحد في الواقع يتوقف على ما هو الغرض من الحد هل هو حصر الذاتيات أو مجرد التمييز كيفما اتفق . أما الارسططاليسيون ـ وغايتهم من الحد حصر الذاتيات ـ فكان الشرط عندهم أن يكون الوصف خاصاً أي يرجع إلى وصف حقيقة المحدود : فالحد إذن « هو القول المفصل المعرف للذات بماهيته »(۲) أما عند الأصوليين ـ وغايتهم من الحد مجرد التمييز ـ فيرجع الحد إلى قول الواصف أي أنه القول المفسر لاسم الحد وصفته عند مستعمله على وجه يخصه ويحصره فلا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو فيه يقول الباقلاني ـ وهو ممثل الفكر الأصولي الأول ـ « الحد هو المحدود بعينه ولو كان غيره لم يكن حده . وإنما على الحاد أن يأتي بعبارة يظن السائل عالماً بها إن جهل ما سأل عنه ، فان جهل العبارات كلها فسحقاً سحقاً »(۳) ولذلك عرف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به ـ والمعرفة كما كان مفهوماً هي العلم . وينكر إمام الحرمين هذا بشدة . ويرى أن الغرض من الحد هو الإشعار بالحقيقة التي بها قيام المسؤول عن حده ، وبه تميزه الذاتي عما عداه ، ولن يوصل تغاير العبارات وإبدال لفظ بلفظ إليها(٤) .
The definition of definition in reality depends on what the purpose of definition is: is it the enumeration of essential properties or mere distinction in any way? As for the Aristotelians—their goal of definition is the enumeration of essential properties—so the condition for them is that the description be specific, i.e., it refers to the description of the reality of the defined. Thus the definition is 'the detailed statement that makes known the self by its quiddity' (2). As for the usuliyyun—their goal of definition is mere distinction—so the definition goes back to the statement of the describer, i.e., it is the explanatory statement of the name of the definition and its attribute according to its user, in a way that specifies and restricts it, so that nothing that is not from it enters, and nothing that is from it goes out. Al-Baqillani—who represents the early usuli thought—says: 'The definition is exactly the defined; if it were other than it, it would not be its definition. It is only incumbent upon the definer to bring a phrase by which he thinks the questioner becomes knowledgeable if he was ignorant of what he asked about. And if he is ignorant of all phrases, then woe, woe.' (3) Therefore, knowledge (al-ʿilm) was defined as the knowledge of the known as it is—and knowledge (al-maʿrifa) as it was understood is al-ʿilm. Imam al-Haramayn strongly rejects this. He holds that the purpose of definition is the indication of the reality by which the thing asked about stands, and by it its intrinsic distinction from other than it is achieved, and variation of expressions and substitution of one word for another will not lead to it (4).
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage delves deeper into the purpose of definition. The author states that the definition of 'definition' depends on whether one aims to enumerate essential properties (essence) or merely to distinguish the thing from others. The Aristotelians aim for the former; thus their definition must be specific to the reality of the defined, such as 'the detailed statement that makes known the self by its quiddity.' The usuliyyun aim for mere distinction; thus the definition is simply an explanatory statement that specifies and restricts the name and attribute, ensuring it excludes what is not part of the thing and includes what is. The author then quotes al-Baqillani, the early usuli thinker, who says the definition is identical to the defined, and the definer's job is to provide a phrase that makes the questioner understand if they were ignorant—if they remain ignorant, there is no hope. Following al-Baqillani, knowledge (al-ʿilm) was defined as 'knowledge of the known as it is,' equating maʿrifa and ʿilm. Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) strongly rejects this view, arguing that the purpose of definition is to indicate the reality that establishes the thing and gives it intrinsic distinction from others. Mere verbal variation, he insists, cannot achieve this. Thus, the passage presents two competing usuli views: al-Baqillani's extreme pragmatism and Juwayni's more essentialist tendency, though still within a pragmatic framework.

Key Terms
حصر الذاتيات
مجرد التمييز
الحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the purpose of definition for the usuliyyun?
A) Ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt (enumeration of essential properties)
B) Mujarrad al-tamyīz (mere distinction)
C) Al-ishʿār bi al-ḥaqīqa (indication of reality)
D) Al-ḥadd huwa al-maḥdūd bi-ʿaynihi (definition is the defined itself)

Key Takeaway
The purpose of definition — essential enumeration or pragmatic distinction — shapes the content and scope of definitions in Islamic logic and usul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Define the term 'wājib' (obligatory) from an Aristotelian perspective (listing essential properties) and from a usuli perspective (mere distinction from mandūb).
?
Identify which purpose the definition of 'ṣiḥḥa' (validity) in the Hanafi school follows: is it ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt or mujarrad al-tamyīz? Give your reasoning.

Al-Baqillani vs. Imam al-Haramayn: Two Usuli Views on Definition
Author contrasts al-Baqillani and Imam al-Haramayn on definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast al-Baqillani's view that 'the definition is exactly the defined' with Imam al-Haramayn's view that definition indicates reality, and evaluate their implications for defining 'ilm.

Core Idea
Two usuli giants disagreed on whether definition is a pragmatic verbal tool or an indicator of true reality.

Within the usuli tradition itself, there was disagreement on the nature of definition. Al-Baqillani took an extreme pragmatic position: the definition is identical to the defined, and the definer's only job is to produce understanding. Imam al-Haramayn (Juwayni) strongly rejected this, insisting that definition must indicate the reality that gives the thing its intrinsic distinction. This lesson unpacks their debate.

Guided Path
1
Find al-Baqillani's view
Locate the quote 'al-ḥadd huwa al-maḥdūd bi-ʿaynihi' and understand it. Then read his statement about the definer's job regarding the questioner's ignorance.
Al-Baqillani believes that if the questioner doesn't understand any phrase, the definer cannot do anything — 'woe, woe'.
2
Understand the definition of 'ilm
Following al-Baqillani, knowledge was defined as 'maʿrifat al-maʿlūm ʿalā mā huwa bihi', equating maʿrifa and ʿilm. This shows the consequence of his view.
This definition is circular if maʿrifa and ʿilm are synonyms.
3
Identify Imam al-Haramayn's objection
He 'strongly rejects' al-Baqillani's view. He argues that the purpose of definition is 'al-ishʿār bi al-ḥaqīqa' — indication of the reality. Changing words alone cannot achieve this.
Juwayni wants definition to capture the real essence, not just produce any understanding.
4
Contrast their underlying purposes
Al-Baqillani's purpose aligns with 'mujarrad al-tamyīz' (mere distinction). Juwayni's purpose leans toward 'ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt' but still within a usuli framework, seeking a middle ground.
This shows that usuli thought is not monolithic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن تعريف الحد في الواقع يتوقف على ما هو الغرض من الحد هل هو حصر الذاتيات أو مجرد التمييز كيفما اتفق . أما الارسططاليسيون ـ وغايتهم من الحد حصر الذاتيات ـ فكان الشرط عندهم أن يكون الوصف خاصاً أي يرجع إلى وصف حقيقة المحدود : فالحد إذن « هو القول المفصل المعرف للذات بماهيته »(۲) أما عند الأصوليين ـ وغايتهم من الحد مجرد التمييز ـ فيرجع الحد إلى قول الواصف أي أنه القول المفسر لاسم الحد وصفته عند مستعمله على وجه يخصه ويحصره فلا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو فيه يقول الباقلاني ـ وهو ممثل الفكر الأصولي الأول ـ « الحد هو المحدود بعينه ولو كان غيره لم يكن حده . وإنما على الحاد أن يأتي بعبارة يظن السائل عالماً بها إن جهل ما سأل عنه ، فان جهل العبارات كلها فسحقاً سحقاً »(۳) ولذلك عرف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به ـ والمعرفة كما كان مفهوماً هي العلم . وينكر إمام الحرمين هذا بشدة . ويرى أن الغرض من الحد هو الإشعار بالحقيقة التي بها قيام المسؤول عن حده ، وبه تميزه الذاتي عما عداه ، ولن يوصل تغاير العبارات وإبدال لفظ بلفظ إليها(٤) .

It is only incumbent upon the definer to bring a phrase by which he thinks the questioner becomes knowledgeable if he was ignorant of what he asked about. And if he is ignorant of all phrases, then woe, woe.' (3) Therefore, knowledge (al-ʿilm) was defined as the knowledge of the known as it is—and knowledge (al-maʿrifa) as it was understood is al-ʿilm. Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage delves deeper into the purpose of definition. The author states that the definition of 'definition' depends on whether one aims to enumerate essential properties (essence) or merely to distinguish the thing from others. The Aristotelians aim for the former; thus their definition must be specific to the reality of the defined, such as 'the detailed statement that makes known the self by its quiddity.' Mere verbal variation, he insists, cannot achieve this. Checkpoint
What does Imam al-Haramayn hold as the purpose of definition?
A) Mujarrad al-tamyīz (mere distinction)
B) Al-ishʿār bi al-ḥaqīqa (indication of reality)
C) Ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt (enumeration of essential properties)
D) Al-ḥadd huwa al-maḥdūd bi-ʿaynihi (definition is the defined itself)

Key Takeaway
Al-Baqillani views definition as a pragmatic tool to produce understanding, while Imam al-Haramayn sees it as an indicator of the thing's reality — a key tension in usuli logic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'ijmāʿ' (consensus) as 'the agreement of the mujtahids of the ummah after the Prophet's death on a religious ruling.' Is this closer to al-Baqillani's or Juwayni's view of definition? Explain.
?
Construct a definition of 'qiyās' (analogy) that would satisfy Juwayni's requirement of indicating reality, and another that would satisfy al-Baqillani's pragmatic requirement.

Purpose of Definition: Distinction vs Essence
Author contrasts Aristotelian and Usuli definitions of hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Aristotelian and Usuli purposes of definition and identify which purpose a given definition serves.

Core Idea
Definition in Islamic legal theory is about distinction, not revealing hidden essences.

The passage contrasts two competing views on the goal of definition: the Aristotelian aim to enumerate essential properties (al-dhatiyyat) and the Usuli aim to merely distinguish (al-tamyiz) the defined from other things. Understanding this difference is crucial for reading classical Islamic texts on definition, especially in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two purposes
Find in the Arabic where the author states the goal of definition for the Aristotelians (ḥaṣr al-dhātiyyāt) and for the usuliyyūn (mujarrad al-tamyīz). Note the contrasting key terms.
The Aristotelians seek to capture the essence; the usuliyyūn seek only to set the defined apart.
2
Examine the conditions each school imposes
For the Aristotelians, the description must be specific to the reality of the defined (qaṣr ʿalā ḥaqīqat al-maḥdūd). For the usuliyyūn, the definition must be an expression that explains the name and attribute in a way that specifies and confines it (yukhaṣṣiṣuhu wa-yaḥṣuruhu). Compare these conditions.
The usuliyyūn allow definition by concomitant properties (al-khawāṣṣ al-lāzima) without requiring genus or differentia.
3
Recognize implications for usul al-fiqh
Think about how a definition of a legal term like 'ribā' would differ under each view. Under the Aristotelian view, you would need to state the essence of ribā; under the Usuli view, you only need to state what makes ribā distinct from other transactions.
This affects how rulings are derived from definitions.

إن الحد عند أرسطو ـ كما رأينا ـ هو المعرف للماهية أو للذات أو بأنه الجواب الصحيح في سؤال ما هو إذا أحاط بالمسؤول عنه » هذه التعاريف كلها ميتافيزيقية لم يقبلها الأصوليون وأوردوا عليها الإعتراضين الآتيين ـ أولهما : أن الحد قد يذكر من غير أن يكون هناك سؤال ـ ثانياً : أن الحد ليس معرفاً للماهية أو موصلاً للسكنة لعسر التوصل إلى هذا أو استحالته . لكن حد الشيء هو معناه الذي لأجله استحق الوصف المقصود(۱) . وهذا اتجاه في تعريف الحد يخالف الحد الأرسططاليسي . وسنوضح هذا توضيحاً أكثر . إن تعريف الحد في الواقع يتوقف على ما هو الغرض من الحد هل هو حصر الذاتيات أو مجرد التمييز كيفما اتفق . أما الارسططاليسيون ـ وغايتهم من الحد حصر الذاتيات ـ فكان الشرط عندهم أن يكون الوصف خاصاً أي يرجع إلى وصف حقيقة المحدود : فالحد إذن « هو القول المفصل المعرف للذات بماهيته »(۲) أما عند الأصوليين ـ وغايتهم من الحد مجرد التمييز ـ فيرجع الحد إلى قول الواصف أي أنه القول المفسر لاسم الحد وصفته عند مستعمله على وجه يخصه ويحصره فلا يدخل فيه ما ليس منه ولا يخرج منه ما هو فيه يقول الباقلاني ـ وهو ممثل الفكر الأصولي الأول ـ « الحد هو المحدود بعينه ولو كان غيره لم يكن حده . وإنما على الحاد أن يأتي بعبارة يظن السائل عالماً بها إن جهل ما سأل عنه ، فان جهل العبارات كلها فسحقاً سحقاً »(۳) ولذلك عرف العلم بأنه معرفة المعلوم على ما هو به ـ والمعرفة كما كان مفهوماً هي العلم . وينكر إمام الحرمين هذا بشدة . ويرى أن الغرض من الحد هو الإشعار بالحقيقة التي بها قيام المسؤول عن حده ، وبه تميزه الذاتي عما عداه ، ولن يوصل تغاير العبارات وإبدال لفظ بلفظ إليها(٤) . ويمكننا أن نستخلص من تعريف أبي الحسين البصري للعام(١) أنه ينحو في الحد أيضاً هذا المنحى اللفظي . فان الحد عنده شرح اسم اللفظ على وجه يخصه ويحصره . ولم يلجأ في تعاريفه إلى أي نوع من أنواع الحدود الارسططاليسية بل إن الحد لدى المتكلمين جميعاً إنما يراد به التمييز بين المحدود وغيره وأنه يحصل بالخواص اللازمة التي لا تحتاج إلى ذكر الصفات المشتركة بينه وبين غيره . يقول ابن تيمية « . . . المحققون من النظار يعلمون أن الحد فائدته التمييز بين المحدود وغيره كالإسم ليس فائدته تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته . وإنما يدعي هذا أهل المنطق اليونانيون أتباع أرسطو ومن سلك سبيلهم وحذا حذوهم تقليداً لهم من الإسلاميين وغيرهم ، فأما جماهير أهل النظر والكلام من المسلمين وغيرهم فعلى خلاف هذا . وإنما أدخل هذا في كلام من تكلم في أصول الدين والفقه بعد أبي حامد في أواخر المائة الخامسة وأوائل السادسة ، وهم الذين تكلموا في الحدود بطريقة أهل المنطق اليوناني . وأما سائر النظار من جميع الطوائف الأشعرية والمعتزلة والكرامية والشيعة وغيرهم ، فعندهم إنما يفيد الحد التمييز بين المحدود وغيره . بل أكثرهم لا يسوغون الحد إلا بما يميز المحدود عن غيره ، ولا يجوز أن يذكر في الحد ما يعم المحدود وغيره ، سواء سمي جنساً أو عرضاً عاماً وإنما يحدون بما يلازم المحدود طرداً وعكساً ولا فرق عندهم بين ما يسمى فصلاً وخاصة ونحو ذلك مما يتميز به المحدود عن غيره . وذلك مشهور في كتب أبي الحسن الأشعري والقاضي أبي بكر وأبي إسحق وابن فورك والقاضي أبي يعلى وابن عقيل والنسفي وأبي علي وأبي هاشم وعبد الجبار والطوسي ومحمد بن الهيثم وغيرهم »(٢) وفي فقرة أخرى يقول « ولم يكن قدماء المتكلمين يرضون أن يخوضوا في الحدود على طريقة المنطقيين كما جد في ذلك متأخروهم »(٣) ويقول صاحب كشاف اصطلاحات(١) الآمدي . الأحكام في أصول الأحكام جـ 2 ص 286 .
Indeed, the definition according to Aristotle – as we have seen – is the identifier of the essence or of the self, or that it is the correct answer to the question 'what is it?' when it encompasses the thing asked about. These definitions are all metaphysical; the usuliyyun did not accept them and raised the following two objections: first, that the definition may be mentioned without there being a question; second, that the definition is not a knower of the essence nor a means to tranquility due to the difficulty or impossibility of reaching this. But the definition of a thing is its meaning for which it deserves the intended description (1). This is a direction in defining definition that contradicts the Aristotelian definition. We will clarify this further. Indeed, the definition of definition in reality depends on what the purpose of definition is: whether it is to enumerate the essential properties or merely distinction in any case. As for the Aristotelians – their goal from definition being enumeration of essentials – the condition for them was that the description be specific, i.e., referring to the description of the reality of the defined: so definition is 'the detailed statement that knows the self by its essence' (2). As for the usuliyyun – their goal from definition being mere distinction – definition goes back to the statement of the describer, i.e., it is the statement that explains the name of the defined and its attribute for its user in a way that specifies and confines it, so that nothing enters it that is not from it and nothing leaves it that is in it. Al-Baqillani – who represents the first usuli thought – says: 'The definition is the defined itself; if it were other than it, it would not be its definition. The definer must only bring an expression by which he thinks the questioner will be knowledgeable if he is ignorant of what he asked about. If he is ignorant of all expressions, then woe, woe' (3). Therefore he defined knowledge as knowing the known as it is – and knowledge as understood was knowledge. Imam al-Haramayn strongly denies this. He holds that the purpose of definition is to indicate the reality by which the thing defined stands, and by which it is essentially distinguished from others, and that changing expressions and substituting one word for another will not lead to it (4). We can deduce from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's definition of 'umum' (1) that he also follows this lexical approach in definition. For definition according to him is the explanation of the name of the word in a way that specifies and confines it. He did not resort in his definitions to any type of Aristotelian definitions; rather, definition according to all theologians is only intended to distinguish between the defined and other things, and it is achieved by necessary properties that do not require mentioning the common attributes between it and others. Ibn Taymiyya says: '... The verifiers among the theorists know that the benefit of definition is distinction between the defined and other things, like the name; its benefit is not to depict the defined and define its reality. This is only claimed by the Greek logicians, followers of Aristotle, and those who follow their path and imitate them out of blind imitation among the Islamics and others. As for the majority of the people of theory and kalam among Muslims and others, they are contrary to this. This was only introduced into the speech of those who spoke in usul al-din and fiqh after Abu Hamid at the end of the fifth century and the beginning of the sixth; they are the ones who spoke about definitions in the manner of Greek logic. As for the rest of the theorists from all groups – Ash'aris, Mu'tazilites, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and others – according to them, definition only benefits distinction between the defined and other things. Rather, most of them do not allow definition except with what distinguishes the defined from others, and it is not permissible to mention in the definition what is common to the defined and others, whether called genus or common accident. They only define by what is concomitant with the defined positively and negatively, and they make no distinction between what is called differentia and special property and the like, by which the defined is distinguished from others. This is well-known in the books of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, Abu Ishaq, Ibn Furak, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, al-Nasafi, Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, 'Abd al-Jabbar, al-Tusi, Muhammad ibn al-Haytham, and others' (2). In another paragraph he says: 'The early theologians were not satisfied with delving into definitions in the manner of the logicians, as the later ones did' (3). And the author of Kashshaf Istilahat says (1) al-Amidi, al-Ahkam fi Usul al-Ahkam, vol. 2, p. 286.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison between the Aristotelian and Usuli (Islamic legal theorists) conceptions of 'hadd' (definition). The author begins by stating that Aristotle's definition – as a statement that reveals the essence or answers 'what is it?' – is metaphysical and was rejected by the usuliyyun on two grounds: first, that definitions can be given without a question, and second, that defining the essence is either difficult or impossible. Instead, the author asserts that a definition is simply the meaning for which a thing deserves a certain description. The passage then explains that the purpose of definition determines its nature: the Aristotelians aim to enumerate the essential properties (al-dhatiyyat) of a thing, so their condition is that the description be specific to the thing's reality. In contrast, the usuliyyun aim merely to distinguish the defined from other things, so the definition is an explanatory statement that specifies the name and attribute of the defined, making it exclusive and comprehensive. The author provides al-Baqillani's view as a representative of early usuli thought: the definition is identical to the defined; its purpose is to provide an expression that informs the questioner. This leads to defining 'knowledge' as 'knowing the known as it is', which Imam al-Haramayn strongly rejects, insisting that the purpose of definition is to indicate the reality by which the defined is essentially distinguished. The author then notes that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri also follows the lexical approach, defining definition as an explanation of the name. Finally, a lengthy quotation from Ibn Taymiyya reinforces the Usuli position: the benefit of definition is distinction, not depiction of reality; Greek logicians are the ones who claim otherwise; the majority of Islamic theologians (Ash'aris, Mu'tazilites, etc.) hold that definitions only distinguish, and they use concomitant properties (lazim) that are true of the defined and nothing else. Ibn Taymiyya also criticizes later theologians who adopted Greek logic after al-Ghazali. This passage thus serves as a critique of Aristotelian logic and a defense of the Usuli approach to definition, emphasizing practical distinction over metaphysical essence.

Checkpoint
According to the Usuli view as presented in the passage, what is the main purpose of a definition (hadd)?
A) To reveal the essence of the defined thing
B) To enumerate all essential properties of the defined
C) To distinguish the defined from other things
D) To provide a metaphysical explanation of the defined

Key Takeaway
In Islamic legal theory, a definition is an expression that distinguishes one thing from others, using concomitant properties, rather than a metaphysical statement of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) from a standard usul al-fiqh textbook (e.g., al-Mustasfa by al-Ghazali). Determine whether it follows the Aristotelian or Usuli approach by checking if it lists essential properties or merely distinguishes qiyas from other sources of law.
?
Write a one-paragraph argument using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning: take a disputed term (e.g., 'maṣlaḥa') and construct a definition that only distinguishes it from similar concepts, deliberately avoiding any claim to reveal its essence.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Aristotelian Definitions
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Aristotelian definition and defense of the Usuli view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's arguments against the Aristotelian definition and explain his alternative view that definition is for distinction.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definition is for distinction, not depiction, and that Greek logic corrupted later Islamic theology after al-Ghazali.

Ibn Taymiyya's lengthy quotation in the passage provides a historical critique: the early theologians (mutakallimun) from all schools rejected the Aristotelian definition and used only concomitant properties. He blames al-Ghazali for introducing Greek logic into usul al-din, which later affected definitions. This lesson unpacks his argument and its implications for the study of Islamic thought.

Guided Path
1
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's main claim
Find in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya states that the benefit of definition is distinction (tamyiz), not depiction (taswir) or definition of reality. He compares definition to a name ('ism).
The key phrase is: 'المحققون من النظار يعلمون أن الحد فائدته التمييز بين المحدود وغيره كالإسم ليس فائدته تصوير المحدود وتعريف حقيقته.'
2
Identify his historical narrative
Ibn Taymiyya claims that the majority of Islamic theologians (Ash'aris, Mu'tazilites, etc.) held the distinction view. He says that Greek logic definitions were introduced after Abu Hamid al-Ghazali (late 5th/early 6th century AH). Note the list of early scholars who did not use Aristotelian definitions.
This narrative challenges the idea that Aristotelian logic was universally accepted in Islamic intellectual history.
3
Compare with the Aristotelian view
Contrast Ibn Taymiyya's statement that definition is like a name with the Aristotelian condition that definition must be 'the detailed statement that knows the self by its essence.' Under Ibn Taymiyya, definition requires only concomitant properties (al-khawass al-lazima) that are true of the defined and nothing else.
This bypasses the need for genus and differentia.

الأحكام في أصول الأحكام جـ 2 ص 286 .
Indeed, the definition according to Aristotle – as we have seen – is the identifier of the essence or of the self, or that it is the correct answer to the question 'what is it?' when it encompasses the thing asked about. These definitions are all metaphysical; the usuliyyun did not accept them and raised the following two objections: first, that the definition may be mentioned without there being a question; second, that the definition is not a knower of the essence nor a means to tranquility due to the difficulty or impossibility of reaching this. But the definition of a thing is its meaning for which it deserves the intended description (1). This is a direction in defining definition that contradicts the Aristotelian definition. We will clarify this further. Indeed, the definition of definition in reality depends on what the purpose of definition is: whether it is to enumerate the essential properties or merely distinction in any case. As for the Aristotelians – their goal from definition being enumeration of essentials – the condition for them was that the description be specific, i.e., referring to the description of the reality of the defined: so definition is 'the detailed statement that knows the self by its essence' (2). As for the usuliyyun – their goal from definition being mere distinction – definition goes back to the statement of the describer, i.e., it is the statement that explains the name of the defined and its attribute for its user in a way that specifies and confines it, so that nothing enters it that is not from it and nothing leaves it that is in it. Al-Baqillani – who represents the first usuli thought – says: 'The definition is the defined itself; if it were other than it, it would not be its definition. The definer must only bring an expression by which he thinks the questioner will be knowledgeable if he is ignorant of what he asked about. If he is ignorant of all expressions, then woe, woe' (3). Therefore he defined knowledge as knowing the known as it is – and knowledge as understood was knowledge. Imam al-Haramayn strongly denies this. He holds that the purpose of definition is to indicate the reality by which the thing defined stands, and by which it is essentially distinguished from others, and that changing expressions and substituting one word for another will not lead to it (4). We can deduce from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's definition of 'umum' (1) that he also follows this lexical approach in definition. For definition according to him is the explanation of the name of the word in a way that specifies and confines it. He did not resort in his definitions to any type of Aristotelian definitions; rather, definition according to all theologians is only intended to distinguish between the defined and other things, and it is achieved by necessary properties that do not require mentioning the common attributes between it and others. Ibn Taymiyya says: '... The verifiers among the theorists know that the benefit of definition is distinction between the defined and other things, like the name; its benefit is not to depict the defined and define its reality. This is only claimed by the Greek logicians, followers of Aristotle, and those who follow their path and imitate them out of blind imitation among the Islamics and others. As for the majority of the people of theory and kalam among Muslims and others, they are contrary to this. This was only introduced into the speech of those who spoke in usul al-din and fiqh after Abu Hamid at the end of the fifth century and the beginning of the sixth; they are the ones who spoke about definitions in the manner of Greek logic. As for the rest of the theorists from all groups – Ash'aris, Mu'tazilites, Karramiyya, Shi'a, and others – according to them, definition only benefits distinction between the defined and other things. Rather, most of them do not allow definition except with what distinguishes the defined from others, and it is not permissible to mention in the definition what is common to the defined and others, whether called genus or common accident. They only define by what is concomitant with the defined positively and negatively, and they make no distinction between what is called differentia and special property and the like, by which the defined is distinguished from others. This is well-known in the books of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, al-Qadi Abu Bakr, Abu Ishaq, Ibn Furak, al-Qadi Abu Ya'la, Ibn 'Aqil, al-Nasafi, Abu 'Ali, Abu Hashim, 'Abd al-Jabbar, al-Tusi, Muhammad ibn al-Haytham, and others' (2). In another paragraph he says: 'The early theologians were not satisfied with delving into definitions in the manner of the logicians, as the later ones did' (3). And the author of Kashshaf Istilahat says (1) al-Amidi, al-Ahkam fi Usul al-Ahkam, vol. 2, p. 286.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison between the Aristotelian and Usuli (Islamic legal theorists) conceptions of 'hadd' (definition). The author begins by stating that Aristotle's definition – as a statement that reveals the essence or answers 'what is it?' – is metaphysical and was rejected by the usuliyyun on two grounds: first, that definitions can be given without a question, and second, that defining the essence is either difficult or impossible. Instead, the author asserts that a definition is simply the meaning for which a thing deserves a certain description. The passage then explains that the purpose of definition determines its nature: the Aristotelians aim to enumerate the essential properties (al-dhatiyyat) of a thing, so their condition is that the description be specific to the thing's reality. In contrast, the usuliyyun aim merely to distinguish the defined from other things, so the definition is an explanatory statement that specifies the name and attribute of the defined, making it exclusive and comprehensive. The author provides al-Baqillani's view as a representative of early usuli thought: the definition is identical to the defined; its purpose is to provide an expression that informs the questioner. This leads to defining 'knowledge' as 'knowing the known as it is', which Imam al-Haramayn strongly rejects, insisting that the purpose of definition is to indicate the reality by which the defined is essentially distinguished. The author then notes that Abu al-Husayn al-Basri also follows the lexical approach, defining definition as an explanation of the name. Finally, a lengthy quotation from Ibn Taymiyya reinforces the Usuli position: the benefit of definition is distinction, not depiction of reality; Greek logicians are the ones who claim otherwise; the majority of Islamic theologians (Ash'aris, Mu'tazilites, etc.) hold that definitions only distinguish, and they use concomitant properties (lazim) that are true of the defined and nothing else. Ibn Taymiyya also criticizes later theologians who adopted Greek logic after al-Ghazali. This passage thus serves as a critique of Aristotelian logic and a defense of the Usuli approach to definition, emphasizing practical distinction over metaphysical essence.

Two Concepts of Definition: Usuli vs. Aristotelian
Author contrasts usuli and Aristotelian definitions, citing scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the usuli and Aristotelian definitions and evaluate which conditions each school imposes.

Core Idea
For usuliyyun, definition distinguishes; for Aristotelians, it conceptualizes essence.

This passage reveals a fundamental disagreement about the purpose of definition. The usuliyyun see definition as a tool for distinguishing one thing from another, while the Aristotelians demand that it capture the essence (mahiyya) through genus and differentia. Understanding this difference is crucial for anyone engaging with Islamic legal theory and philosophical logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions of hadd
Locate where the author defines hadd for the usuliyyun (as synonymous with mu'arraf, distinguishing a thing from others) and for the Aristotelians (as producing a form in the mind not previously present).
These definitions are stated early in the passage.
2
Compare the goals: distinction vs. conceptualization
Notice that the usuli definition aims at tamyiz (distinction), while the Aristotelian aims at taswir (conceptualization) of the essence. The author quotes al-Zarkashi and Imam al-Haramayn to emphasize this contrast.
Imam al-Haramayn notes the theologians' goal is 'distinction by the specific property'.
3
Examine the conditions each school imposes
For Aristotelians, a proper definition requires genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) in correct order. For usuliyyun, the condition is ittirād wa in'ikās (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness). Find the passage where Imam al-Haramayn states that these are necessary for a complete usuli definition.
The passage also notes that some usuliyyun deny essence altogether, while others accept a mental essence.
4
Understand the debate over ease and difficulty
Read the quotes from Ibn Sina, Abu al-Barakat al-Baghdadi, al-Razi, and Ibn Taymiyya. They argue that if definition aims at essence, it is extremely difficult; if it merely details the name, it is easy. The author sides with the usuli view of ease.
This debate highlights the practical implications for Islamic sciences.
