The Usuli Objection: A Differentia Can Accompany Two Genera
Author presents the second Usuli objection: a differentia can be associated with two genera in composite essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Usuli position on the association of a differentia with two genera and contrast it with the Aristotelian objection based on causality.

Core Idea
A single differentia can serve as such for multiple genera depending on comparative context.

In this lesson, we explore the second objection raised by the Usuliyyun against the Aristotelian restriction that a differentia can only accompany one genus. Using the example of 'animal' and 'white', we see how a single term can function as either genus or differentia depending on the essence being considered. This challenges the causal link assumed by the Aristotelians.

Guided Path
1
Examine the composite essence example
The author uses 'animal' and 'white' as two limits each more general than the other from one aspect. Note how animal applies to white and others, and white applies to animal and others. This overlapping generality is key.
This is a simple conceptual example.
2
Identify the shifting roles
In the composite essence of white animal, when compared to black animal, animal is the genus and white is the differentia. When compared to white inanimate, white becomes the genus and animal the differentia. So each term can be either.
This shows the flexibility of genus and differentia.
3
Understand the Usuli conclusion
From this, the Usuliyyun conclude that a differentia (e.g., white) can accompany two genera: animal and horse (as mentioned). Similarly, animal as differentia accompanies black and white.
This directly contradicts the Aristotelian restriction.
4
Analyze the Aristotelian objection
The Aristotelians argue that a differentia, being a cause, can only be associated with a single genus. If it accompanied two genera at the same level, then one essence would have two genera, leading to the effect (the composite) lagging behind its cause (the differentia).
This objection is based on a causal principle.
5
Evaluate the debate
Consider whether the Aristotelian causal principle is necessary or if the Usuli perspective is more flexible for defining composite essences.
This is a foundational issue in the logic of definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً ـ أباح الأصوليون « اقتران الفصل بجنسين » وذلك في الماهية المركبة من حدين ، كل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه كالحيوان والأبيض ، فالحيوان يصدق على الأبيض وغيره ، والأبيض يصدق على الحيوان وغيره . فإذا تكونت الماهية منهما كان الحيوان جنساً لها والأبيض فصلاً بالنسبة إلى الحيوان الأسود وبالعكس بالنسبة إلى الجماد الأبيض ـ وقد ذكرنا هذا ـ وينتج من هذا أن الفصل وهو الأبيض في الحالة الأولى يقارن جنسين هما الحيوان والجواد ، كما أن الحيوان إذا اعتبرناه فصلاً يقارن جنسين هما الأسود والأبيض(٢) أما الأرسططاليسيون فيذهبون إلى أن الفصل من حيث هو علة ، لا يقارن إلا جنساً واحداً . فإنه لو قارن جنسين في مرتبة واحدة حتى يكون من الفصل وأحد الجنسين ماهية ومن الجنس الآخر ماهية لاستحالة أن يكون لماهية واحدة جنسان في مرتبة واحدة لزم تخلف المعلول عن علته . وهذا ناتج من وجود الفصل في كل واحدة من هاتين الماهيتين وعدم جنس ما لزمها في الماهية الأخرى .
Second – The Usuliyyun permitted 'the association of a differentia with two genera,' such as in an essence composed of two limits, each of which is more general than the other from one aspect, like animal and white. For animal applies to white and to others, and white applies to animal and to others. So if an essence is formed from them, then animal is a genus for it and white is a differentia relative to black animal, and conversely relative to white inanimate object – and we have mentioned this. And it results from this that the differentia, which is white in the first case, accompanies two genera: animal and horse; just as animal, if we consider it a differentia, accompanies two genera: black and white. As for the Aristotelians, they go that the differentia, as it is a cause, does not accompany except one genus. For if it accompanied two genera at one level such that from the differentia and one of the genera an essence is formed and from the other genus another essence, then it is impossible for one essence to have two genera at one level, which would necessitate the effect lagging behind its cause. And this results from the existence of the differentia in each of these two essences and the absence of a genus that accompanies it in the other essence.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents the second Usuli objection: a differentia can be associated with two different genera in a composite essence. The Usuliyyun give the example of the concepts 'animal' and 'white'. Each is more general than the other in different respects: 'animal' applies to white things and non-white things; 'white' applies to animals and non-animals. If we combine them to form an essence (e.g., a white animal), we can consider 'animal' as the genus and 'white' as the differentia when comparing to a black animal. Conversely, comparing to a white inanimate object, 'white' becomes the genus and 'animal' becomes the differentia. Thus, the same term (e.g., 'white') can function as a differentia in one relation and as a genus in another, and it can accompany two genera (animal and horse) depending on the perspective. The Aristotelians, however, argue that the differentia, because it is a cause, can only accompany one genus. If it accompanied two genera at the same level, then from the differentia and one genus we would have one essence, and from the other genus another essence. But it is impossible for a single essence to have two genera at the same level, because that would imply that the effect (the composite essence) lags behind its cause (the differentia) – a logical contradiction. The Aristotelians reason that the differentia exists in each of the two essences, but there is no genus that accompanies it in both essences, leading to inconsistency.

Key Terms
اقتران الفصل بجنسين
الماهية المركبة

Key Takeaway

Third Objection: A Differentia Introducing Two Species
Author presents the third Usuli objection on differentia producing two species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Usuli argument that a differentia associated with two genera can produce two species, and contrast it with the Aristotelian position.

Core Idea
If a differentia can accompany two genera, it logically produces two species.

This third objection is a direct consequence of the second. The Usuliyyun draw out a necessary implication: if a differentia can attach to two genera, it must give rise to two distinct species. This challenges the Aristotelian principle of a one-to-one correspondence between differentia, genus, and species. Understanding this dispute reveals deeper assumptions about the nature of definitions and their role in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Usuli Claim
Find the statement in the Arabic: 'إذا كان يجوز اقتران الفصل بجنسين , فانه يكون مقدماً لنوعين'. This is a conditional claim: if permission is granted, the consequence follows.
The claim is that the differentia becomes productive of two species.
2
Trace the Logical Consequence
Understand the necessity: if a differentia can pair with two genera, it defines two distinct species, one under each genus. For example, 'white' differentia with 'animal' gives 'white animal', and with 'body' gives 'white body'.
This challenges the Aristotelian one-to-one mapping.
3
Understand the Aristotelian Counter
Aristotelians hold that a differentia is associated with only one genus and thus constitutes only one species. They view the differentia as having a causal, unique link to its genus.
Their position is based on ontological causation, not mere logical possibility.
4
Compare the Underlying Assumptions
Usuliyyun treat the differentia as a logical predicate that can combine freely; Aristotelians treat it as an essential determinant. This difference affects how definitions are constructed in Islamic sciences.
The Usuli position allows more flexibility in defining terms across disciplines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً ـ يرى الأصوليون أنه إذا كان يجوز اقتران الفصل بجنسين , فانه يكون مقدماً لنوعين وهذه نتيجة لازمة . أما الأرسططاليسيون فيرون أنه إذا كان الفصل لا يقترن إلا بجنس واحد فهو لا يقوم إلا نوعاً واحداً(٣) .
Third – The Usuliyyun hold that if the association of a differentia with two genera is permissible, then it will be productive of two species, and this is a necessary consequence. As for the Aristotelians, they hold that if the differentia is not associated except with one genus, then it does not constitute except one species.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This brief passage presents the third objection, which is a direct corollary of the second. The Usuliyyun argue that since a differentia can accompany two genera (as shown in the previous point), it follows that the same differentia can be the basis for two different species. For example, 'white' as a differentia can combine with 'animal' to form one species (white animal) and with 'body' to form another species (white body). This is logically necessary given their permission of dual association. The Aristotelians counter that a differentia, being a cause of only one genus, can only give rise to one species. They maintain a one-to-one correspondence: one differentia, one genus, one species. This highlights the divergent ontological commitments: the Aristotelians see the differentia as having a causal role that uniquely determines a single species, while the Usuliyyun see it as a logical predicate that can enter multiple compositions.

Key Terms
النوع
الفصل
الجنس

A) Because a differentia can have multiple definitions.
B) Because it can associate with two genera.
C) Because species are independent of genera.
D) Because Aristotelians allow it.

Key Takeaway
The Usuliyyun argue that if a differentia can accompany two genera, it necessarily produces two species, challenging the Aristotelian one-to-one mapping.

Fourth Objection: Multiple Differentiae for One Species
Author presents the fourth Usuli objection: multiple differentiae can exist for a single species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Usuli argument for multiple differentiae and the Aristotelian response based on the principle of one cause per effect.

Core Idea
The Usuliyyun see no contradiction in multiple differentiae; the Aristotelians uphold a strict one-cause-one-effect principle.

This lesson examines a key debate between Islamic legal theorists (Usuliyyun) and Aristotelian logicians over whether a species can have more than one differentia. The Usuliyyun allow a hierarchy of differentiae, while the Aristotelians insist on a single proximate differentia to avoid two causes for one effect. Understanding this debate is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate the Usuli view (allowing multiple differentiae) and the Aristotelian view (only one proximate differentia). Note the terms used: 'أجاز الأصوليون تكثر الفصول' and 'الأرسططاليسيون ... يقررون أن الفصل القريب لا يكون إلا واحداً'.
2
Extract the Aristotelian argument
The Aristotelians argue that if multiple differentiae existed, it would mean 'توارد علتين على معلول واحد بالذات' – two causes concurring on a single effect essentially. Understand why this is considered impossible: an effect cannot have two independent causes for the same aspect.
3
Understand the Usuli rebuttal
The Usuliyyun accept a hierarchical multiplicity: e.g., first differentia = rational, second = animal, third = body. They see these as ordered, not conflicting causes. Recognize that the disagreement hinges on the definition of 'cause' and whether differentiae are causal.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Consider the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal'. The differentia 'rational' is the proximate differentia. But can we add 'animal' as a differentia? According to Aristotelians, no – only one proximate differentia. According to Usuliyyun, yes – it is a higher-order differentia.
This illustrates the practical difference between the two schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً ـ أجاز الأصوليون تكثر الفصول فيوجد فصل أول وثان وثالث ورابع(٤) أما الأرسططاليسيون فعلى العكس من ذلك يقررون أن الفصل القريب لا يكون إلا واحداً فانه لو تعدد , لزم توارد علتين على معلول واحد بالذات . . . والفصل لا تجوز زيادته على واحد لأنه يقوم وجود حصة النوع من
Fourth – The Usuliyyun allowed the multiplicity of differentiae, so there is a first, second, third, and fourth differentia. As for the Aristotelians, on the contrary, they affirm that the proximate differentia is only one; for if it were multiple, it would necessitate the concurrence of two causes on one effect essentially... and the differentia cannot be increased beyond one because it establishes the existence of the species' portion from...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection: the Usuliyyun permit multiple differentiae (fasl) - a first, second, third, and fourth - for a single species, while the Aristotelians insist on a single proximate differentia. The Aristotelian reasoning is that if there were multiple differentiae, two causes (the differentiae) would concur on one effect (the species) in an essentially causal manner, which is impossible because one effect cannot have two independent causes for the same aspect. Additionally, the differentia is what constitutes the specific portion (hissat al-naw') of the species, and that portion can only be constituted by one differentia. The text is cut off but the core contrast is clear: the Usuliyyun accept a hierarchical multiplicity of differentiae (e.g., first differentia = 'rational', second = 'animal', third = 'body'), whereas the Aristotelians maintain that only the immediate (proximate) differentia is relevant for defining the species.

Key Takeaway
The debate over multiple differentiae hinges on whether differentiae are treated as causes of the species; Aristotelians reject multiplicity to avoid causal overdetermination, while Usuliyyun accept hierarchical multiplicity.

?

?

Three Key Disagreements on the Differentia
Author enumerates three disputes between usuliyun and Aristotelians on the differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to contrast the usuli and Aristotelian positions on the number of genera a differentia can associate with, its resulting species, and its multiplicity, and explain the logical rationale behind each position.

Core Idea
The differentia is not as fixed as Aristotle thought: usuliyun see it as multi-genus and multi-species in function.

In this passage, the author presents three specific disagreements between Islamic legal theorists (usuliyun) and Aristotelians concerning the nature of the differentia (fasl). Understanding these disagreements reveals how Islamic logic adapted Aristotelian categories to better serve the needs of usul al-fiqh and definitional precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three points of disagreement
Scan the Arabic passage for the numbered points: ثانياً (second), ثالثاً (third), رابعاً (fourth). Each introduces a distinct disagreement between the usuliyun and the Aristotelians.
The first point is not included in this passage, but the numbering shows there were earlier disputes.
2
Understand the usuli position on each point
For each point, extract what the usuliyun allow or hold: Second—they permit the differentia to associate with two genera. Third—if allowed, it causes two species. Fourth—they permit multiple differentiae (first, second, third, etc.).
3
Understand the Aristotelian counter-position
For each point, extract the Aristotelian view: Second—the differentia can only associate with one genus because it is a cause. Third—with one genus, it causes only one species. Fourth—the proximate differentia is only one; what appears as multiple are actually parts of one differentia.
4
Analyze the underlying logical rationale
For the second point, the Aristotelian argument is that if a differentia associated with two genera at one level, one quiddity would have two genera, causing the effect to lag behind the cause. For the fourth point, multiple differentiae would imply two causes for one effect, which is impossible. Capture these causal notions.
The usuli view is more permissive, taking composite quiddities (e.g., animal and white) as examples.
5
Apply to a real fiqh definition
Consider the definition of 'salah' as 'specific words and actions, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. According to the usuli view, 'words and actions' could be a composite quiddity with multiple differentiae. According to the Aristotelian view, the entire set of actions might be considered one differentia with parts.
This shows how the disagreement affects real definitions in Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً ـ أباح الأصوليون « اقتران الفصل بجنسين » وذلك في الماهية المركبة من حدين ، كل واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه كالحيوان والأبيض ، فالحيوان يصدق على الأبيض وغيره ، والأبيض يصدق على الحيوان وغيره . فإذا تكونت الماهية منهما كان الحيوان جنساً لها والأبيض فصلاً بالنسبة إلى الحيوان الأسود وبالعكس بالنسبة إلى الجماد الأبيض ـ وقد ذكرنا هذا ـ وينتج من هذا أن الفصل وهو الأبيض في الحالة الأولى يقارن جنسين هما الحيوان والجواد ، كما أن الحيوان إذا اعتبرناه فصلاً يقارن جنسين هما الأسود والأبيض(٢) أما الأرسططاليسيون فيذهبون إلى أن الفصل من حيث هو علة ، لا يقارن إلا جنساً واحداً . فإنه لو قارن جنسين في مرتبة واحدة حتى يكون من الفصل وأحد الجنسين ماهية ومن الجنس الآخر ماهية لاستحالة أن يكون لماهية واحدة جنسان في مرتبة واحدة لزم تخلف المعلول عن علته . وهذا ناتج من وجود الفصل في كل واحدة من هاتين الماهيتين وعدم جنس ما لزمها في الماهية الأخرى . ثالثاً ـ يرى الأصوليون أنه إذا كان يجوز اقتران الفصل بجنسين ، فانه يكون مقدماً لنوعين وهذه نتيجة لازمة . أما الأرسططاليسيون فيرون أنه إذا كان الفصل لا يقترن إلا بجنس واحد فهو لا يقوم إلا نوعاً واحداً(٣) . رابعاً ـ أجاز الأصوليون تكثر الفصول فيوجد فصل أول وثان وثالث ورابع(٤) أما الأرسططاليسيون فعلى العكس من ذلك يقررون أن الفصل القريب لا يكون إلا واحداً فانه لو تعدد ، لزم توارد علتين على معلول واحد بالذات . . . والفصل لا تجوز زيادته على واحد لأنه يقوم وجود حصة النوع من الجنس ، فان كفى الواحد في التقويم استغنى عن الآخر ، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً . وحيث وجد في كلام العلماء تعدد الفصول كقولهم فصل ثان وثالث فلا تحقيق في هذه العبارة ، بل المجموع فصل واحد وكل واحد مما جعلوه فصلاً هو جزء الفصل
Second: The legal theorists permitted 'the association of the differentia with two genera' — this is in a quiddity composed of two limits, each of which is more general than the other from one aspect, such as 'animal' and 'white'. For 'animal' is true of white and other things, and 'white' is true of animal and other things. When the quiddity is constituted from them, 'animal' is a genus for it and 'white' is a differentia relative to the black animal, and vice versa relative to the white inanimate object — and we have mentioned this. And it results from this that the differentia, which is 'white' in the first case, is associated with two genera, i.e., 'animal' and 'substance', just as 'animal' if we consider it as a differentia is associated with two genera, i.e., 'black' and 'white'. As for the Aristotelians, they hold that the differentia, insofar as it is a cause, is associated with only one genus. For if it were associated with two genera at one level, such that a quiddity comes from the differentia and one genus and a quiddity from the other genus — because it is impossible for one quiddity to have two genera at one level — the effect would lag behind its cause. And this results from the existence of the differentia in each of these two quiddities and the absence of some genus that necessarily accompanies it in the other quiddity. Third: The legal theorists hold that if the association of the differentia with two genera is permitted, then it would be a cause for two species, and this is a necessary result. As for the Aristotelians, they hold that if the differentia is associated with only one genus, then it constitutes only one species. Fourth: The legal theorists permitted the multiplicity of differentiae, so there is a first, second, third, and fourth differentia. As for the Aristotelians, to the contrary, they affirm that the proximate differentia is only one. For if it were multiple, two causes would necessarily concur upon one effect per se... And the differentia cannot be increased beyond one because it constitutes the existence of the portion of the species from the genus. For if one suffices for constitution, the other is dispensed with; otherwise, it would not be a differentia. And wherever multiplicity of differentiae is found in the speech of scholars, like their saying 'second differentia' and 'third', there is no verification in this expression; rather, the totality is one differentia, and each of what they made a differentia is a part of the differentia.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents four points of disagreement between Islamic legal theorists (usuliyun) and Aristotelians regarding the differentia (fasl). It is a comparative exposition. The author begins with the second point (following a first point not included here): the usuliyun allow the differentia to be associated with two genera within a composite quiddity. The example is the quiddity composed of 'animal' and 'white' — here 'animal' is genus from one perspective and 'white' is differentia, but from another perspective 'white' could be genus for a white inanimate object, so the differentia (white) is associated with two genera: animal and substance. The Aristotelians object, arguing that the differentia as cause can only associate with one genus; otherwise, the same quiddity would have two genera simultaneously, causing the effect to lag behind its cause. The third point: if association with two genera is allowed, the differentia would cause two species; Aristotelians counter that with one genus it causes only one species. The fourth point: usuliyun permit multiple differentiae (first, second, etc.), while Aristotelians insist on only one proximate differentia; multiplicity would entail two causes for one effect. Finally, the author reports the Aristotelian rebuttal: what scholars call multiple differentiae are actually parts of one differentia.

C) The differentia cannot associate with any genus.
D) The differentia associates with exactly one genus for each species.

Key Takeaway
The differentia is more flexible in Islamic logic: it can associate with multiple genera and appear as multiple parts, reflecting the needs of fiqh definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task 1: Take the definition of 'riba' as 'an increase in a specific exchange without equivalent consideration'. Identify the genus and differentia according to the usuli view. Then check whether the differentia could be associated with more than one genus from different perspectives.

Critique of Considerative Quiddity as a Foundation for Usuli Definition
Author critiques the usuli reliance on considerative quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author argues that considerative quiddity cannot serve as the basis for usuli definition, and identify the two key factors underlying this problem.

Core Idea
The entire problem of usuli definition hinges on whether quiddity is real or a mental construct.

In this passage, the author dissects the foundational problem of usuli definition theory. He identifies two interrelated issues: the definition of the differentia and the theologians' view of quiddity as a purely mental 'considerative' entity. Some Muslim thinkers, like Ibn Hazm, were drawn to the Aristotelian framework of genus and differentia but rejected grounding definitions on this considerative quiddity. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pillars of the problem
Locate in the Arabic where the author states that the entire problem is based on two things: defining the reality of the differentia and the considerative quiddity.
The phrase 'المشكلة كلها تقوم على تعريف حقيقة الفصل واختلاف وجهة الأصوليين والأرسططاليسيين... وعلى اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية'.
2
Understand considerative quiddity
Define al-mahiyya al-i'tibariyya as an essence that exists only conceptually, not in external reality.
This contrasts with Aristotelian real quiddity.
3
Examine Ibn Hazm's response
Ibn Hazm insisted on precise definition of terms but did not adopt considerative quiddity; he used genus and differentia.
He wrote a special chapter on terms current among theorists.
4
Connect to the inductive nature
The author later argues that usuli logic is inductive and empirical, making fixed quiddity inappropriate.
This step previews the next lesson.

وفي الواقع إن المشكلة كلها تقوم على تعريف حقيقة الفصل واختلاف وجهة الأصوليين والأرسططاليسيين في النظر إليها من ناحية ـ ومن ناحية أخرى على اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية . ولم يوافق بعض المفكرين القلائل على استناد الحد الأصولي إلى الماهية الاعتبارية - واستهوتهم فكرة الجنس والفصل ، ولذلك حاولوا إقامة حدودهم عليها ـ فنرى ابن حزم يبحث في فصل خاص « الألفاظ الدائرة بين أهل النظر » وهو يرى أنه يجب تحديد الألفاظ الأصولية ، لأن الخطأ كثيراً ما يحدث وتضيع الحقائق لتشابك المعاني في إيقاع الاسماء على غير مسمياتها . ثم يحدد بعد ذلك تعريف كل لفظ من تلك الألفاظ فيبحث في الحد والرسم والعلم والبرهان والدليل والحجة والأصل والفرع (۲)، وهذا الاتجاه نجده أيضاً لدى إمام الحرمين . وقد يكون مما دعا الغزالي فيما بعد إلى مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلوم المسلمين الالتباس الذي وجده في استخدام الاصطلاحات النظرية . . . ولم يفهم هؤلاء المفكرون قط أنه في منطق استقرائي وتجريبي كالمنطق الإسلامي الأصولي لا يمكن قط الأخذ بفكرة الماهية الثابتة كأساس للحد . . . هذه فكرة قبلها المسلمون من قبل . وسنرى جون استيوارت مل يأخذ بها في منطقه . كما أن رسل يرددها أيضاً . نعود إلى مسألة أخيرة وهي مصادر مبحث الحد الأصولي . أو بمعنى أضيق هل كان تفكير الأصوليين في مبحث الحد خاصاً ، أم أن مصادر يونانية أثرت عليهم . أي هل أثرت عليهم الرواقية ؟ ليست هناك أدنى صلة بين المبحثين لسببين : أن مبحث الحد الرواقي
And in reality, the entire problem is based on the definition of the reality of the differentia and the difference of perspective between the legal theorists and the Aristotelians in looking at it on one hand, and on the other hand on the theologians' consideration of quiddity as considerative. And a few thinkers did not agree with basing the usuli definition on considerative quiddity — they were attracted by the idea of genus and differentia, and so they tried to establish their definitions on it. Thus we see Ibn Hazm investigating in a special chapter 'The terms current among the people of theoretical investigation', and he holds that the usuli terms must be defined, because error often occurs and truths are lost due to the intertwining of meanings in placing names on other than their named things. Then he defines each of those terms, investigating definition, description, knowledge, demonstration, proof, argument, root, and branch. And we also find this direction with Imam al-Haramayn. And perhaps what called al-Ghazali later to mix Aristotelian logic with the Islamic sciences was the confusion he found in the use of theoretical terminology... These thinkers never understood that in an inductive and empirical logic like Islamic usuli logic, one can never adopt the idea of fixed quiddity as a basis for definition... This is an idea that Muslims accepted before. And we will see John Stuart Mill adopting it in his logic, and Russell also echoes it. We return to a final issue: the sources of the usuli definition topic. Or in a narrower sense: was the usuli thinking in the topic of definition unique, or did Greek sources influence them? That is, did Stoicism influence them? There is no connection whatsoever between the two topics for two reasons: that the Stoic topic of definition
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage shifts from the earlier enumeration of disagreements to a critical analysis of the foundation of usuli definition theory. The author first states that the entire problem rests on two pillars: (1) the definition of the differentia and the differing perspectives of usuliyun and Aristotelians, and (2) the theologians' treatment of quiddity as 'considerative' (i'tibariyya), i.e., a mental construct rather than a real essence. This second point is key: some thinkers, such as Ibn Hazm and Imam al-Haramayn, rejected grounding definitions on considerative quiddity, yet they were attracted to the formal framework of genus and differentia. The author cites Ibn Hazm's work where he insists on defining terms precisely to avoid error, listing key logical terms (hadd, rasm, 'ilm, burhan, etc.). Imam al-Haramayn follows a similar path. The author then speculates that this confusion over theoretical terminology may have motivated al-Ghazali to integrate Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences. However, the author argues that these thinkers missed a crucial point: in an inductive, empirical logic (like usuli logic), the idea of a fixed, real quiddity cannot serve as the foundation for definition. Yet this idea was later taken up by John Stuart Mill and Bertrand Russell in their logics. Finally, the author addresses the question of sources: whether the usuli definition theory was indigenous or influenced by Greek thought, specifically Stoicism. The passage ends abruptly, stating there is no connection for two reasons, but the text is cut off.

Key Takeaway
The problem of usuli definition reduces to whether quiddity is a real essence or a mental construct; considerative quiddity cannot ground definition in an inductive logic.

The Inductive-Empirical Nature of Usuli Logic and Its Implications for Definition
Author argues that usuli logic is inductive and cannot use fixed quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author claims that usuli logic is inductive and empirical, and why this makes a fixed quiddity inappropriate as a basis for definition.

Core Idea
Usuli logic is inductive, not essentialist; fixed quiddity has no place in it.

The author continues his critique by arguing that thinkers like Ibn Hazm and Imam al-Haramayn did not fully grasp that usuli logic is essentially inductive and empirical, not deductive and essentialist like Aristotelian logic. Therefore, the idea of a fixed, real quiddity cannot serve as the foundation for definition. This lesson examines that claim and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Find the key sentence about Ghazali
Locate where the author says what called al-Ghazali to mix Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences was the confusion over theoretical terminology.
'وقد يكون مما دعا الغزالي فيما بعد إلى مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلوم المسلمين الالتباس الذي وجده في استخدام الاصطلاحات النظرية'.
2
Identify the author's characterization of usuli logic
The author says that in an inductive and empirical logic like Islamic usuli logic, one cannot adopt the idea of fixed quiddity.
'في منطق استقرائي وتجريبي كالمنطق الإسلامي الأصولي لا يمكن قط الأخذ بفكرة الماهية الثابتة كأساس للحد'.
3
Understand the contrast with Aristotelian logic
Aristotelian logic relies on real essences; usuli logic is based on empirical observation and case-by-case reasoning.
The author sees the two as incompatible.
4
Consider the broader implication
If quiddity is not fixed, then definitions must be flexible and context-dependent, which is a major difference from the Aristotelian tradition.
This explains why some thinkers rejected considerative quiddity.

وفي الواقع إن المشكلة كلها تقوم على تعريف حقيقة الفصل واختلاف وجهة الأصوليين والأرسططاليسيين في النظر إليها من ناحية ـ ومن ناحية أخرى على اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية . ولم يوافق بعض المفكرين القلائل على استناد الحد الأصولي إلى الماهية الاعتبارية - واستهوتهم فكرة الجنس والفصل ، ولذلك حاولوا إقامة حدودهم عليها ـ فنرى ابن حزم يبحث في فصل خاص « الألفاظ الدائرة بين أهل النظر » وهو يرى أنه يجب تحديد الألفاظ الأصولية ، لأن الخطأ كثيراً ما يحدث وتضيع الحقائق لتشابك المعاني في إيقاع الاسماء على غير مسمياتها . ثم يحدد بعد ذلك تعريف كل لفظ من تلك الألفاظ فيبحث في الحد والرسم والعلم والبرهان والدليل والحجة والأصل والفرع (۲)، وهذا الاتجاه نجده أيضاً لدى إمام الحرمين . وقد يكون مما دعا الغزالي فيما بعد إلى مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلوم المسلمين الالتباس الذي وجده في استخدام الاصطلاحات النظرية . . . ولم يفهم هؤلاء المفكرون قط أنه في منطق استقرائي وتجريبي كالمنطق الإسلامي الأصولي لا يمكن قط الأخذ بفكرة الماهية الثابتة كأساس للحد . . . هذه فكرة قبلها المسلمون من قبل . وسنرى جون استيوارت مل يأخذ بها في منطقه . كما أن رسل يرددها أيضاً . نعود إلى مسألة أخيرة وهي مصادر مبحث الحد الأصولي . أو بمعنى أضيق هل كان تفكير الأصوليين في مبحث الحد خاصاً ، أم أن مصادر يونانية أثرت عليهم . أي هل أثرت عليهم الرواقية ؟ ليست هناك أدنى صلة بين المبحثين لسببين : أن مبحث الحد الرواقي
And in reality, the entire problem is based on the definition of the reality of the differentia and the difference of perspective between the legal theorists and the Aristotelians in looking at it on one hand, and on the other hand on the theologians' consideration of quiddity as considerative. And a few thinkers did not agree with basing the usuli definition on considerative quiddity — they were attracted by the idea of genus and differentia, and so they tried to establish their definitions on it. Thus we see Ibn Hazm investigating in a special chapter 'The terms current among the people of theoretical investigation', and he holds that the usuli terms must be defined, because error often occurs and truths are lost due to the intertwining of meanings in placing names on other than their named things. Then he defines each of those terms, investigating definition, description, knowledge, demonstration, proof, argument, root, and branch. And we also find this direction with Imam al-Haramayn. And perhaps what called al-Ghazali later to mix Aristotelian logic with the Islamic sciences was the confusion he found in the use of theoretical terminology... These thinkers never understood that in an inductive and empirical logic like Islamic usuli logic, one can never adopt the idea of fixed quiddity as a basis for definition... This is an idea that Muslims accepted before. And we will see John Stuart Mill adopting it in his logic, and Russell also echoes it. We return to a final issue: the sources of the usuli definition topic. Or in a narrower sense: was the usuli thinking in the topic of definition unique, or did Greek sources influence them? That is, did Stoicism influence them? There is no connection whatsoever between the two topics for two reasons: that the Stoic topic of definition
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage shifts from the earlier enumeration of disagreements to a critical analysis of the foundation of usuli definition theory. The author first states that the entire problem rests on two pillars: (1) the definition of the differentia and the differing perspectives of usuliyun and Aristotelians, and (2) the theologians' treatment of quiddity as 'considerative' (i'tibariyya), i.e., a mental construct rather than a real essence. This second point is key: some thinkers, such as Ibn Hazm and Imam al-Haramayn, rejected grounding definitions on considerative quiddity, yet they were attracted to the formal framework of genus and differentia. The author cites Ibn Hazm's work where he insists on defining terms precisely to avoid error, listing key logical terms (hadd, rasm, 'ilm, burhan, etc.). Imam al-Haramayn follows a similar path. The author then speculates that this confusion over theoretical terminology may have motivated al-Ghazali to integrate Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences. However, the author argues that these thinkers missed a crucial point: in an inductive, empirical logic (like usuli logic), the idea of a fixed, real quiddity cannot serve as the foundation for definition. Yet this idea was later taken up by John Stuart Mill and Bertrand Russell in their logics. Finally, the author addresses the question of sources: whether the usuli definition theory was indigenous or influenced by Greek thought, specifically Stoicism. The passage ends abruptly, stating there is no connection for two reasons, but the text is cut off.

الماهية الثابتة

B) It is inductive and empirical

D) It is a copy of Stoic logic

Key Takeaway
Islamic usuli logic is inductive and empirical, therefore it cannot accommodate fixed quiddity as a basis for definition.

The Differentia (Fasl) in Islamic Hadd
Author critiques Aristotelian hadd and argues Islamic hadd is original.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Islamic view of the differentia (fasl) in definition and distinguish it from the Aristotelian view.

Core Idea
In Islamic hadd, multiple differentiae are actually parts of a single differentia.

The passage begins by addressing a technical issue in definition theory: the multiplicity of differentiae. When scholars speak of a 'second differentia' or 'third', the author argues this is not real multiplicity but rather parts of one differentia. This reflects a deeper divergence in how legal theorists and Aristotelians understand the role of the differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about multiple differentiae
The passage states that when scholars say 'second differentia' or 'third', there is no verification in such expressions. Find this claim in the Arabic.
2
Understand the author's reasoning
The author says the whole is one differentia and each part is a part of the differentia. This means multiple differentiae are not independent but components of a single concept.
3
Relate to the difference in perspective
The problem rests on differing views of the reality of the differentia between legal theorists and Aristotelians, and on the theologians' view of essence as mental.
This step connects the specific claim to the broader divergence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجنس ، فان كفى الواحد في التقويم استغنى عن الآخر ، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً . وحيث وجد في كلام العلماء تعدد الفصول كقولهم فصل ثان وثالث فلا تحقيق في هذه العبارة ، بل المجموع فصل واحد وكل واحد مما جعلوه فصلاً هو جزء الفصل . وفي الواقع إن المشكلة كلها تقوم على تعريف حقيقة الفصل واختلاف وجهة الأصوليين والأرسططاليسيين في النظر إليها من ناحية ـ ومن ناحية أخرى على اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية . ولم يوافق بعض المفكرين القلائل على استناد الحد الأصولي إلى الماهية الاعتبارية - واستهوتهم فكرة الجنس والفصل ، ولذلك حاولوا إقامة حدودهم عليها ـ فنرى ابن حزم يبحث في فصل خاص « الألفاظ الدائرة بين أهل النظر » وهو يرى أنه يجب تحديد الألفاظ الأصولية ، لأن الخطأ كثيراً ما يحدث وتضيع الحقائق لتشابك المعاني في إيقاع الاسماء على غير مسمياتها . ثم يحدد بعد ذلك تعريف كل لفظ من تلك الألفاظ فيبحث في الحد والرسم والعلم والبرهان والدليل والحجة والأصل والفرع ، وهذا الاتجاه نجده أيضاً لدى إمام الحرمين . وقد يكون مما دعا الغزالي فيما بعد إلى مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلوم المسلمين الالتباس الذي وجده في استخدام الاصطلاحات النظرية . . . ولم يفهم هؤلاء المفكرون قط أنه في منطق استقرائي وتجريبي كالمنطق الإسلامي الأصولي لا يمكن قط الأخذ بفكرة الماهية الثابتة كأساس للحد . . . هذه فكرة قبلها المسلمون من قبل . وسنرى جون استيوارت مل يأخذ بها في منطقه . كما أن رسل يرددها أيضاً . نعود إلى مسألة أخيرة وهي مصادر مبحث الحد الأصولي . أو بمعنى أضيق هل كان تفكير الأصوليين في مبحث الحد خاصاً ، أم أن مصادر يونانية أثرت عليهم . أي هل أثرت عليهم الرواقية ؟ ليست هناك أدنى صلة بين المبحثين لسببين : أن مبحث الحد الرواقي مختلف فيه هل يقوم على فكرة الماهية كالحد الأرسططاليسي أم لا يقوم . يحاول الاستاذ هاملان Hamelin أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي يقوم على فكرة الماهية ويحاول الأستاذ بروشارد أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي لا يقوم على تلك الفكرة ، ومن ثمة فهو مستقل عن الحد الأرسططاليسي . إذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الأول لم تكن هناك صلة بين الحد الرواقي والحد الأصولي وإذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الثاني كانت هناك بعض الصلة . ولكن هذه الصلة ينفيها نفياً باتاً إنكار الأصوليين لفكرة العلية ـ ونقدهم للحد وغير الحد من مباحث منطق أرسطو على هذا الأساس ، أما الرواقيون فقد اعترفوا بالعلية المنطقية ، وكان لها أثر كبير في فلسفتهم وفي منطقهم على الخصوص . فالمسلمون إذن صدروا في هذا المبحث عن فكر إبتداعي خالص يتفق تمام الاتفاق مع بقية عناصر منطقهم الاسلامي ويخالف الحد الأرسططاليسي كما رأينا في تعريفه ، وفي تكوينه ، وفي طريقة اكتشافه ، وفي جوهره
The genus, for if one suffices for definition, it dispenses with the other, otherwise it is not a differentia. And where a multiplicity of differentiae is found in the speech of scholars, such as their saying 'second differentia' and 'third', there is no verification in this expression; rather the whole is one differentia and each thing they made a differentia is a part of the differentia. In reality, the entire problem rests on defining the reality of the differentia and the difference of perspective between the legal theorists and the Aristotelians in considering it on one hand, and on the other hand the theologians' consideration of essence (mahiyya) as mental. And some few thinkers did not agree to base the legal definition on mental essence, and they were attracted by the idea of genus and differentia, so they tried to establish their definitions upon it. So we see Ibn Hazm discussing in a special section 'the terms current among theoreticians' and he holds that it is necessary to define the legal terms because error often occurs and truths are lost due to the intertwining of meanings in placing names upon other than their referents. Then afterwards he defines each of those terms, investigating definition (hadd), description (rasm), knowledge (ilm), demonstration (burhan), proof (dalil), argument (hujja), principle (asl), and branch (far'). We also find this trend with Imam al-Haramayn. And perhaps what prompted al-Ghazālī later to mix Aristotelian logic with the sciences of the Muslims was the confusion he found in the use of theoretical terms. These thinkers never understood that in an inductive and empirical logic like Islamic legal logic, it is absolutely impossible to adopt the idea of fixed essence as a basis for definition. This is an idea that Muslims had previously accepted. And we will see John Stuart Mill adopt it in his logic, and Russell also repeats it. We return to a final question: the sources of the topic of legal definition. Or more narrowly, was the thinking of the legal theorists on the topic of definition particular, or did Greek sources influence them? That is, did Stoicism influence them? There is no connection whatsoever between the two topics for two reasons: the Stoic topic of definition is disputed—does it rest on the idea of essence like the Aristotelian definition or does it not? Professor Hamelin attempts to prove that the Stoic definition rests on the idea of essence, and Professor Brochard attempts to prove that the Stoic definition does not rest on that idea, and therefore it is independent of the Aristotelian definition. If we adopt the first opinion, there is no connection between the Stoic definition and the legal definition; if we adopt the second opinion, there is some connection. But this connection is absolutely denied by the legal theorists' rejection of the idea of causality and their critique of definition and other topics of Aristotelian logic on this basis. As for the Stoics, they acknowledged logical causality, and it had a great impact on their philosophy and especially on their logic. So the Muslims, in this topic, proceeded from a purely innovative thought, fully consistent with the rest of the elements of their Islamic logic and opposing the Aristotelian definition, as we saw in its definition, its composition, its method of discovery, and its essence.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the concept of 'hadd' (definition) in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) compared to Aristotelian and Stoic logic. The author begins by addressing the issue of the differentia (fasl) in definitions, noting that what scholars sometimes call multiple differentiae are actually parts of a single differentia. The core problem lies in the differing views of legal theorists and Aristotelians on the nature of the differentia, and the theologians' view that essence (mahiyya) is mental rather than real. Some thinkers like Ibn Hazm attempted to base definitions on genus and differentia, as seen in his work where he defines key terms such as hadd, rasm, burhan, etc. This trend continued with Imām al-Haramayn and influenced al-Ghazālī's integration of Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences. However, the author argues that these thinkers missed the fundamental point: Islamic logic is inductive and empirical, based on legal reasoning, and cannot adopt the Aristotelian notion of a fixed, transcendent essence. The author then examines the possible Stoic influence on Islamic definition theory. He notes a scholarly debate: some see the Stoic definition as essence-based (like Aristotle), others see it as independent. If it is independent, there might be a link. But even then, the author dismisses any significant influence because Islamic legal theorists reject the concept of logical causality ('illiyya), while Stoics embrace it. Therefore, the author concludes that the Islamic concept of definition is a purely innovative creation, entirely consistent with the rest of Islamic logic and opposed to Aristotelian definition in its definition, composition, method of discovery, and essence.

الجنس
الماهية

C) They indicate confusion.
D) They are essential in Aristotelian logic.

Key Takeaway
The differentia in Islamic legal definition is a single, unified concept even when expressed as multiple parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a legal definition (hadd) of fasting (sawm) as 'abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse from dawn to sunset with the intention of worship', identify the differentia (fasl) and explain why it is considered a single differentia despite having multiple components.
?

The Mental Essence (Mahiyya I'tibariyya) in Islamic Hadd
Author critiques Aristotelian hadd and argues Islamic hadd is original.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the theologians' view of mahiyya as mental construct shapes the Islamic concept of definition.

Core Idea
Islamic hadd is based on mental considerations, not on a fixed external essence.

The passage identifies a key factor in the divergence between Islamic and Aristotelian definitions: the theologians consider mahiyya (essence) as mental construct (i'tibari), not as a real external quiddity. This metaphysical stance has profound implications for how definitions are formulated and validated in Islamic legal theory.

Guided Path
1
Locate the mention of mahiyya i'tibariyya
Find where the passage says 'the theologians' consideration of essence as mental'.
This appears in the sentence: 'اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية'
2
Understand the contrast with Aristotelian mahiyya
Aristotelians treat mahiyya as a real, external quiddity that definitions aim to capture. The author contrasts this with the Islamic view where mahiyya is mental.
3
Analyze the implications for definition
If mahiyya is mental, definitions are not about discovering real essences but about constructing conceptual tools for legal reasoning. This makes Islamic hadd instrumental and inductive.
This is crucial for understanding why Islamic logic rejects fixed essences.

The author begins by addressing the issue of the differentia (fasl) in definitions, noting that what scholars sometimes call multiple differentiae are actually parts of a single differentia. The core problem lies in the differing views of legal theorists and Aristotelians on the nature of the differentia, and the theologians' view that essence (mahiyya) is mental rather than real. Some thinkers like Ibn Hazm attempted to base definitions on genus and differentia, as seen in his work where he defines key terms such as hadd, rasm, burhan, etc. This trend continued with Imām al-Haramayn and influenced al-Ghazālī's integration of Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences. However, the author argues that these thinkers missed the fundamental point: Islamic logic is inductive and empirical, based on legal reasoning, and cannot adopt the Aristotelian notion of a fixed, transcendent essence. The author then examines the possible Stoic influence on Islamic definition theory. He notes a scholarly debate: some see the Stoic definition as essence-based (like Aristotle), others see it as independent. If it is independent, there might be a link. But even then, the author dismisses any significant influence because Islamic legal theorists reject the concept of logical causality ('illiyya), while Stoics embrace it. Therefore, the author concludes that the Islamic concept of definition is a purely innovative creation, entirely consistent with the rest of Islamic logic and opposed to Aristotelian definition in its definition, composition, method of discovery, and essence.

Ibn Hazm and the Genus-Differentia Approach
Author critiques Aristotelian hadd and argues Islamic hadd is original.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate Ibn Hazm's attempt to base definitions on genus and differentia and understand why the author considers it a misunderstanding.

Core Idea
Ibn Hazm and others tried to adopt Aristotelian genus-differentia, but missed the inductive nature of Islamic logic.

The passage mentions that a few thinkers, like Ibn Hazm, were attracted to the idea of genus and differentia and attempted to establish definitions on that model. Ibn Hazm dedicated a section to defining key terms such as hadd, rasm, burhan, etc. Similarly, Imām al-Haramayn followed this trend. The author suggests this may have influenced al-Ghazālī's integration of Aristotelian logic. However, the author argues these thinkers fundamentally misunderstood the nature of Islamic logic—it is inductive and empirical, not essentialist.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thinkers mentioned
The passage names Ibn Hazm, Imām al-Haramayn, and implicitly al-Ghazālī. Note what each contributed.
Ibn Hazm defined legal terms using genus-differentia; al-Ghazālī mixed Aristotelian logic with Islamic sciences.
2
Understand their approach
They wanted to base Islamic definitions on genus and differentia, following the Aristotelian pattern.
Ibn Hazm's work on 'al-alfadh al-da'ira bayn ahl al-nazar' is cited.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author says they never understood that Islamic logic is inductive and cannot adopt fixed essences. He calls their attempt a failure to grasp the essential difference.
This critique is part of the larger argument for the originality of Islamic hadd.

الجنس ، فان كفى الواحد في التقويم استغنى عن الآخر ، وإلا لم يكن فصلاً . وحيث وجد في كلام العلماء تعدد الفصول كقولهم فصل ثان وثالث فلا تحقيق في هذه العبارة ، بل المجموع فصل واحد وكل واحد مما جعلوه فصلاً هو جزء الفصل . وفي الواقع إن المشكلة كلها تقوم على تعريف حقيقة الفصل واختلاف وجهة الأصوليين والأرسططاليسيين في النظر إليها من ناحية ـ ومن ناحية أخرى على اعتبار المتكلمين الماهية إعتبارية . ولم يوافق بعض المفكرين القلائل على استناد الحد الأصولي إلى الماهية الاعتبارية - واستهوتهم فكرة الجنس والفصل ، ولذلك حاولوا إقامة حدودهم عليها ـ فنرى ابن حزم يبحث في فصل خاص « الألفاظ الدائرة بين أهل النظر » وهو يرى أنه يجب تحديد الألفاظ الأصولية ، لأن الخطأ كثيراً ما يحدث وتضيع الحقائق لتشابك المعاني في إيقاع الاسماء على غير مسمياتها . ثم يحدد بعد ذلك تعريف كل لفظ من تلك الألفاظ فيبحث في الحد والرسم والعلم والبرهان والدليل والحجة والأصل والفرع ، وهذا الاتجاه نجده أيضاً لدى إمام الحرمين . وقد يكون مما دعا الغزالي فيما بعد إلى مزج المنطق الأرسططاليسي بعلوم المسلمين الالتباس الذي وجده في استخدام الاصطلاحات النظرية . . . ولم يفهم هؤلاء المفكرون قط أنه في منطق استقرائي وتجريبي كالمنطق الإسلامي الأصولي لا يمكن قط الأخذ بفكرة الماهية الثابتة كأساس للحد . . . هذه فكرة قبلها المسلمون من قبل . وسنرى جون استيوارت مل يأخذ بها في منطقه . كما أن رسل يرددها أيضاً . نعود إلى مسألة أخيرة وهي مصادر مبحث الحد الأصولي . أو بمعنى أضيق هل كان تفكير الأصوليين في مبحث الحد خاصاً ، أم أن مصادر يونانية أثرت عليهم . أي هل أثرت عليهم الرواقية ؟ ليست هناك أدنى صلة بين المبحثين لسببين : أن مبحث الحد الرواقي مختلف فيه هل يقوم على فكرة الماهية كالحد الأرسططاليسي أم لا يقوم . يحاول الاستاذ هاملان Hamelin أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي يقوم على فكرة الماهية ويحاول الأستاذ بروشارد أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي لا يقوم على تلك الفكرة ، ومن ثمة فهو مستقل عن الحد الأرسططاليسي . إذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الأول لم تكن هناك صلة بين الحد الرواقي والحد الأصولي وإذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الثاني كانت هناك بعض الصلة . ولكن هذه الصلة ينفيها نفياً باتاً إنكار الأصوليين لفكرة العلية ـ ونقدهم للحد وغير الحد من مباحث منطق أرسطو على هذا الأساس ، أما الرواقيون فقد اعترفوا بالعلية المنطقية ، وكان لها أثر كبير في فلسفتهم وفي منطقهم على الخصوص . فالمسلمون إذن صدروا في هذا المبحث عن فكر إبتداعي خالص يتفق تمام الاتفاق مع بقية عناصر منطقهم الاسلامي ويخالف الحد الأرسططاليسي كما رأينا في تعريفه ، وفي تكوينه ، وفي طريقة اكتشافه ، وفي جوهره
The genus, for if one suffices for definition, it dispenses with the other, otherwise it is not a differentia. And where a multiplicity of differentiae is found in the speech of scholars, such as their saying 'second differentia' and 'third', there is no verification in this expression; rather the whole is one differentia and each thing they made a differentia is a part of the differentia. In reality, the entire problem rests on defining the reality of the differentia and the difference of perspective between the legal theorists and the Aristotelians in considering it on one hand, and on the other hand the theologians' consideration of essence (mahiyya) as mental. And some few thinkers did not agree to base the legal definition on mental essence, and they were attracted by the idea of genus and differentia, so they tried to establish their definitions upon it. So we see Ibn Hazm discussing in a special section 'the terms current among theoreticians' and he holds that it is necessary to define the legal terms because error often occurs and truths are lost due to the intertwining of meanings in placing names upon other than their referents. Then afterwards he defines each of those terms, investigating definition (hadd), description (rasm), knowledge (ilm), demonstration (burhan), proof (dalil), argument (hujja), principle (asl), and branch (far'). We also find this trend with Imam al-Haramayn. And perhaps what prompted al-Ghazālī later to mix Aristotelian logic with the sciences of the Muslims was the confusion he found in the use of theoretical terms. These thinkers never understood that in an inductive and empirical logic like Islamic legal logic, it is absolutely impossible to adopt the idea of fixed essence as a basis for definition. This is an idea that Muslims had previously accepted. And we will see John Stuart Mill adopt it in his logic, and Russell also repeats it. We return to a final question: the sources of the topic of legal definition. Or more narrowly, was the thinking of the legal theorists on the topic of definition particular, or did Greek sources influence them? That is, did Stoicism influence them? There is no connection whatsoever between the two topics for two reasons: the Stoic topic of definition is disputed—does it rest on the idea of essence like the Aristotelian definition or does it not? Professor Hamelin attempts to prove that the Stoic definition rests on the idea of essence, and Professor Brochard attempts to prove that the Stoic definition does not rest on that idea, and therefore it is independent of the Aristotelian definition. If we adopt the first opinion, there is no connection between the Stoic definition and the legal definition; if we adopt the second opinion, there is some connection. But this connection is absolutely denied by the legal theorists' rejection of the idea of causality and their critique of definition and other topics of Aristotelian logic on this basis. As for the Stoics, they acknowledged logical causality, and it had a great impact on their philosophy and especially on their logic. So the Muslims, in this topic, proceeded from a purely innovative thought, fully consistent with the rest of the elements of their Islamic logic and opposing the Aristotelian definition, as we saw in its definition, its composition, its method of discovery, and its essence.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the concept of 'hadd' (definition) in Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) compared to Aristotelian and Stoic logic. The author begins by addressing the issue of the differentia (fasl) in definitions, noting that what scholars sometimes call multiple differentiae are actually parts of a single differentia. The core problem lies in the differing views of legal theorists and Aristotelians on the nature of the differentia, and the theologians' view that essence (mahiyya) is mental rather than real. Some thinkers like Ibn Hazm attempted to base definitions on genus and differentia, as seen in his work where he defines key terms such as hadd, rasm, burhan, etc. This trend continued with Imām al-Haramayn and influenced al-Ghazālī's integration of Aristotelian logic into Islamic sciences. However, the author argues that these thinkers missed the fundamental point: Islamic logic is inductive and empirical, based on legal reasoning, and cannot adopt the Aristotelian notion of a fixed, transcendent essence. The author then examines the possible Stoic influence on Islamic definition theory. He notes a scholarly debate: some see the Stoic definition as essence-based (like Aristotle), others see it as independent. If it is independent, there might be a link. But even then, the author dismisses any significant influence because Islamic legal theorists reject the concept of logical causality ('illiyya), while Stoics embrace it. Therefore, the author concludes that the Islamic concept of definition is a purely innovative creation, entirely consistent with the rest of Islamic logic and opposed to Aristotelian definition in its definition, composition, method of discovery, and essence.

Stoic Influence on Islamic Hadd: A Debate
Author critiques Aristotelian hadd and argues Islamic hadd is original.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to assess the possible Stoic influence on Islamic definition theory and explain why the author denies such influence.

Core Idea
Stoic influence on Islamic hadd is denied due to the rejection of logical causality by legal theorists.

The passage turns to the question of sources: Did Stoic logic influence Islamic definition theory? The author presents a scholarly debate between Hamelin and Brochard on whether the Stoic definition was based on essence. Even if it was not, the author argues that any connection is negated by the Islamic rejection of logical causality ('illiyya), which the Stoics accepted. Therefore, the Islamic concept of definition is purely innovative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scholarly opinions on Stoic definition
Hamelin argues Stoic definition is based on essence (like Aristotle); Brochard argues it is not. Understand both positions.
2
Consider the implications for Islamic-Stoic connection
If Hamelin is right, no connection; if Brochard is right, there might be some connection. The author then introduces a decisive factor.
3
Analyze the decisive factor: causality
Islamic legal theorists reject logical causality ('illiyya), while Stoics embrace it. This fundamental disagreement severs any possible influence.
4
Conclude on the originality of Islamic hadd
The author concludes that Muslims proceeded from a purely innovative thought, consistent with Islamic logic and opposed to the Aristotelian one.

The Stoic Definition Debate and Its Implications for Islamic Logic
Scholarly debate on Stoic definition and its link to Islamic definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two scholarly positions on the Stoic definition (essence-based vs. independent) and describe how the usuliyyun's rejection of causality severs any link between Stoic and Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
The usuliyyun's rejection of logical causality makes the Islamic definition fully original.

This passage presents a modern scholarly debate on whether the Stoic definition is based on essence like the Aristotelian. Two scholars, Hamelin and Brochard, offer contrasting views. The implications for Islamic logic are significant: the usuliyyun's rejection of causality cuts off any connection, affirming the originality of the Islamic definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Locate where Hamelin argues that the Stoic definition is based on the idea of essence, and where Brochard argues it is not, making it independent of the Aristotelian definition.
This is a historical debate imported from Western scholarship on Stoic logic.
2
Trace the implications for Islamic logic
Understand that if Hamelin is correct, there is no connection between Stoic and usuli definitions; if Brochard is correct, there is some connection.
3
Recognize the usuliyyun's stance on causality
The passage states that the usuliyyun absolutely deny any connection because they reject the idea of logical causality (al-ʿilliyya), which both Aristotelian and Stoic definitions presuppose.
This rejection is a key feature of classical usul al-fiqh methodology.
4
Conclude on the originality of Islamic logic
The author concludes that Islamic definition is a purely creative thought, differing from Aristotelian definition in essence, composition, and method.
This underscores the independence of Islamic intellectual tradition.

مختلف فيه هل يقوم على فكرة الماهية كالحد الأرسططاليسي أم لا يقوم . يحاول الاستاذ هاملان Hamelin أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي يقوم على فكرة الماهية ويحاول الأستاذ بروشارد أن يثبت أن الحد الرواقي لا يقوم على تلك الفكرة ، ومن ثمة فهو مستقل عن الحد الأرسططاليسي. إذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الأول لم تكن هناك صلة بين الحد الرواقي والحد الأصولي وإذا ما أخذنا بالرأي الثاني كانت هناك بعض الصلة . ولكن هذه الصلة ينفيها نفياً باتاً إنكار الأصوليين لفكرة العلية ـ ونقدهم للحد وغير الحد من مباحث منطق أرسطو على هذا الأساس ، أما الرواقيون فقد اعترفوا بالعلية المنطقية ، وكان لها أثر كبير في فلسفتهم وفي منطقهم على الخصوص . فالمسلمون إذن صدروا في هذا المبحث عن فكر إبتداعي خالص يتفق تمام الاتفاق مع بقية عناصر منطقهم الاسلامي ويخالف الحد الأرسططاليسي كما رأينا في تعريفه ، وفي تكوينه ، وفي طريقة اكتشافه ، وفي جوهره وسنتابع في الفصل القادم ، الجزء الثاني من هذا المنطق الإسلامي .
It is disputed whether it is based on the idea of essence like the Aristotelian definition or not. Professor Hamelin attempts to prove that the Stoic definition is based on the idea of essence, and Professor Brochard attempts to prove that the Stoic definition is not based on that idea, and therefore it is independent of the Aristotelian definition. If we take the first opinion, there is no connection between the Stoic definition and the usuli definition; if we take the second opinion, there is some connection. But this connection is absolutely denied by the usuliyyun's rejection of the idea of causality—and their critique of the definition and other topics of Aristotelian logic on this basis. As for the Stoics, they acknowledged logical causality, and it had a great impact on their philosophy and their logic in particular. So the Muslims, in this subject, originated a purely creative thought, fully consistent with the rest of the elements of their Islamic logic and differing from the Aristotelian definition as we have seen in its definition, composition, method of discovery, and essence. We will continue in the next chapter, the second part of this Islamic logic.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage discusses the historical debate about the nature of the Stoic definition and its relation to the Islamic (usuli) definition. The author presents two scholarly positions: Hamelin argues that the Stoic definition is based on the idea of essence (like the Aristotelian), while Brochard argues it is not, making it independent. The author then examines the implications: if Hamelin is right, there is no link between Stoic and usuli definitions; if Brochard is right, there is some link. However, the author emphasizes that the usuliyyun (Islamic scholars of usul) categorically deny any such link because they reject the concept of logical causality (al-ʿilliyya) and critique Aristotelian logic on that basis. In contrast, the Stoics accepted logical causality. The conclusion is that the Islamic definition is a purely original product, differing from the Aristotelian in its essence, composition, and method. This highlights the independence and creativity of Islamic logic.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason the usuliyyun deny a connection between Stoic and usuli definitions?
A) They reject the idea of logical causality.
B) They prefer Aristotelian definitions.
C) They consider Stoic definitions too complex.
D) They had no access to Stoic texts.

Key Takeaway
The Islamic (usuli) definition is an original creation, independent of both Aristotelian and Stoic definitions, due to the rejection of logical causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Compare the usuli definition of 'qiyas' (as found in al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa) with the Aristotelian and Stoic definitions described in the passage. Identify one key difference that stems from the rejection of causality.
?
Write a short paragraph explaining why the usuliyyun's critique of Aristotelian logic, mentioned in the passage, is essential for understanding the originality of Islamic logic.
